Sources of Dialectical Premises
Author classifies the permissible sources of dialectical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify dialectical premises by their source and give examples of disagreements among philosophers and the public.

Core Idea
Dialectical premises are drawn from non-absolute well-known opinions or from areas of disagreement.

After defining the dialectical question, the author turns to its permissible sources. Not every premise is suitable; only those that are not absolutely certain but still commonly held or debated among experts. This classification is important for choosing appropriate premises in a debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of sources
The text says a dialectical question can be from 'non-absolute well-known premises' or 'others'. The 'others' include philosophical opinions not commonly held, disagreements between philosophers and the public, among philosophers, or among the public.
2
Analyze the example of pleasure
The author gives the example of pleasure: the public think it is good, but philosophers do not. This illustrates a disagreement between philosophers and the public, making it a suitable source for a dialectical premise.
The example shows that the premise 'pleasure is good' is not absolutely well-known but is still usable in dialectic.
3
List all possible disagreement types
The text mentions: what philosophers do not hold as well-known, disagreement between philosophers and others, disagreement among philosophers themselves, and disagreement among the public themselves. Each type provides premises that are not fully settled.
4
Understand why these sources are permissible
Dialectic aims to persuade a specific audience using premises that are acceptable to them. Therefore, premises that are common or debated are more effective than absolute truths or obscure claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالتي هي الأولى بأن تكون مسألة جدلية أي أن تكون مقدمة تؤخذ على سبيل المسألة فهي ما يكون طلب التسليم فيها لمعني ينتفع في إثبات مطلوب من باب ما يوثر أو يجتنب، أو مطلوب اعتقادي من باب ما يرى حقا ويقصد فيه المعرفة: فيكون إما أن يبلغ بتسلمه الغرض في ذلك، أو بأن يجعل مقدّمة تُعِينُ في إنتاج ما تؤخذ مقدّمة أو يكون معينا على ذلك بأن يكون قانونًا منطقيًا ينفع منفعة المنطق. ويكون إما عن المشهورات الغير المطلقة وإما عن غيرها؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا اعتقاد فيه للفلاسفة اعتقادًا مشهورًا أو من جملة ما فيه اختلاف بين الفلاسفة وبين غيرهم كما في اللذة، فإن الجمهور ذهبوا إلى أن اللذة خير، والفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إليه، أو يكون فيه للفلاسفة فيما بينهم اختلاف أو يكون فيه للجمهور فيما بينهم اختلاف.
In summary, that which is primarily fit to be a dialectical question—i.e., a premise taken in the manner of a question—is that which seeks acceptance for a meaning that is useful in proving a desired conclusion from the category of what is preferred or avoided, or a doctrinal desired conclusion from the category of what is considered true and for which knowledge is intended. It may either achieve the goal by its acceptance, or serve as a premise that helps in producing what is taken as a premise, or be an aid to that by being a logical rule beneficial for logic. And it may be from non-absolute well-known premises or from others; this includes what the philosophers do not hold as a well-known belief, or what involves disagreement between philosophers and others, as in the case of pleasure—for the public hold that pleasure is good, while the philosophers do not hold that—or it may involve disagreement among the philosophers themselves, or disagreement among the public themselves.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition of a dialectical question (mas'ala jadaliyya). The author states that a dialectical question is a premise that is put forward as a request for acceptance. The purpose of seeking acceptance is to use it in proving a desired conclusion (matlub). The desired conclusion may be practical (relating to what is preferred or avoided, such as ethical actions) or theoretical (relating to what is considered true, aiming at knowledge). The premise can serve in three ways: (1) its mere acceptance directly achieves the goal, (2) it helps produce another premise that leads to the goal, or (3) it is a logical principle that aids reasoning. The source of the premise can be non-absolute mashhurat (well-known premises that are not universally accepted) or other types, such as philosophical opinions that are not commonly held, or points of disagreement between philosophers and the public, among philosophers themselves, or among the public themselves. The example of pleasure illustrates: the public generally believes pleasure is good, but philosophers disagree. This shows how a dialectical question may rely on opinions that are not absolutely well-known but are still suitable for dialectical debate. The classification helps students understand the range of premises permissible in dialectic.

Key Terms
المشهورات الغير المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an example of a source for a dialectical question?
A) An axiom of logic accepted by all.
B) A disagreement among philosophers themselves.
C) A verse from the Quran with clear meaning.
D) A mathematical proof.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical premises can come from non-absolute well-known opinions or from various types of disagreement, making them suitable for persuasive debates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a premise from a real Islamic debate (e.g., 'Miracles are possible'). Classify its source according to the author's categories: is it from non-absolute mashhurat, or does it involve a specific type of disagreement? Provide your reasoning.

Distinguishing Matlub from Wad' in Dialectic
Author clarifies matlub is not a postulate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between matlub (sought proposition) and wad' (postulate) in dialectic, and explain why a matlub is not inherently a wad'.

Core Idea
The sought proposition is never a postulate by itself, but may become one when disputed.

In dialectical argument, precision in terminology is crucial. The author of this passage refines the understanding of two key terms: 'matlub' (the proposition to be proved) and 'wad'' (postulate). While they can overlap in certain contexts, they are fundamentally distinct. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its significance for rigorous debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Terms
Locate the terms 'matlub' and 'wad'' in the Arabic text. Note their definitions as given in the passage.
Matlub is the sought proposition; wad' is a postulate or claim.
2
Extract the Main Claim
The author states: 'المطلوب من حيث هو المطلوب لا يكون وضعا' – the matlub as such is not a wad'. This is the core distinction.
The matlub does not come with built-in acceptance; it requires proof.
3
Understand the Exception
The author adds that a wad' can become matlub in some respects, specifically when there is disagreement among early authorities (al-awail). Then any opinion, relative to its holder, is a wad' (claim).
This nuance prevents over-generalization.
4
Distinguish Dialectical from Demonstrative Wad'
The author clarifies that the dialectical use of 'wad'' differs from its technical meaning in the Book of Demonstration (Posterior Analytics). In demonstration, a wad' is an unproven claim; in dialectic, it is any opinion relative to its holder.
The similarity is only by analogy, not identity.
5
Analyze the Final Distinction
The passage ends by separating mere claims (da'awa) from genuine postulates (awda') in the strict sense. This sharpens the logical tool for dialecticians.
Understanding this prevents confusion across disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن المطلوب من حيث هو المطلوب لا يكون وضعا وإن كان الوضع قد يصير مطلوبا بوجه ما كما إذا وقع فيه للأوائل خلاف والرأي أي رأي كان فإنه بالقياس إلى باصره وضع أي دعوى؛ وليس ما تسميته في كتاب البرهان وضعا أعني الدعوى الذي لا يكون مؤيد الحجة بصحته. وإنما سمي ذلك وضعا لمناسبة ما بينه وبين الوضع هنا؛ فإن من الدعاوي ما هو دعوى فقط، ومن الأوضاع ما هو وضع فقط.
And know that the sought proposition, insofar as it is sought, is not a postulate; although the postulate may become sought in some respect, as when there is disagreement among the early authorities about it. And any opinion whatsoever, in relation to its holder, is a postulate – that is, a claim. But what you call a postulate in the Book of Demonstration is not that, namely a claim that is not supported by a proof of its truth. Rather, it is called a postulate due to a similarity between it and the postulate here; for among claims there is that which is merely a claim, and among postulates there is that which is merely a postulate.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage refines the understanding of the term 'matlub' (sought proposition) in dialectic. The author states that the matlub is not inherently a 'wad'' (postulate). However, a postulate can sometimes become a matlub if there is disagreement among early authorities (al-awail). In that case, any opinion, relative to its holder, is considered a postulate (a claim). But this use of 'wad'' is different from its technical meaning in the Book of Demonstration (likely Aristotle's Posterior Analytics). In demonstration, a wad' is a claim not supported by proof. The author explains that the term is used in dialectic by analogy, not in the strict sense. The final sentence distinguishes between mere claims (da'awa) and postulates (awda') in the strict sense. This guards against confusing the dialectical use of 'wad'' with the demonstrative one, ensuring precision in logical terminology.

Key Terms
المطلوب
الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the matlub is not a wad' in dialectic?
A) Because the matlub is always supported by proof.
B) Because the matlub is only a claim, not a postulate.
C) Because the matlub is not assumed without proof; but it may become a wad' in cases of disagreement.
D) Because the wad' is only used in demonstration.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, the matlub is not inherently a postulate; it becomes one only when questioned, and the dialectical wad' is an extension of the demonstrative concept by analogy, not a precise equivalent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a dialectical exchange, your opponent says: 'The prohibition of this contract is a matlub I will prove.' Your co-debater whispers: 'But that is just a wad'.' Using the author's distinction, explain whether the opponent's statement is correctly labelled as wad'.
?
Task 2: Read the following claim from a classical usul text: 'The consensus of the Companions is a proof.' Would this be considered a matlub or a wad' in a dialectical context? Justify your answer with reference to the passage's criteria.

The Two Foundations of Dialectical Argument
Author classifies argument into syllogism and induction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish syllogism from induction as the two types of dialectical argument and explain their respective strengths in Islamic dialectical debates.

Core Idea
Syllogism compels the intellect; induction persuades the masses.

In this opening of a new chapter, the author turns from the components of dialectical speech (predicates, premises, sought propositions) to the argument itself (al-hujja). He classifies all arguments into two types: syllogism (qiyas) and induction (istiqra'), and immediately compares their epistemic strengths. This distinction is crucial for any dialectician who must choose the appropriate tool for the audience and purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching category
Find where the author defines the argument (al-hujja) as the 'ultimate composition' of dialectical speech. Note that he treats it as the final element in the sequence.
The argument is what dialectical speech aims at.
2
Extract the two types
The author states 'كل حجة إما قياس وإما استقراء' – every argument is either a syllogism or an induction. Identify these as the only two species of hujja.
There is no third type in classical dialectic.
3
Compare their characteristics
The author contrasts them: syllogism is 'closer to the intellect and more compelling', induction is 'closer to sense perception and more persuasive' and has more impact on the public. Paraphrase these differences in your own words.
Compelling (ilzam) vs. persuasive (iqna').
4
Relate to dialectical practice
Consider why a dialectician might prefer induction when addressing a lay audience, and syllogism when addressing scholars. This is a strategic choice in actual debates.
The choice depends on the opponent and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في الآلات التي تتم بها ملكة الجدل وطلب المواضع وهي أربع ولما مر الكلام في بسط الأمور الجدلية وهي المحمولات، وما يليها وهو الذي منه الكلام الجدلي أعني المقدّمات، وما فيه الكلام أعني المطالب؛ فلا بد من النظر في نفس الكلام الجدلي وهو الذي إليه غاية التركيب وهو الحجة. فنقول كل حجة إمـا قياس وإما استقراء. والقياس أقرب إلى العقل وأشد إلزاما، والاستقراء أقرب إلى الحس وأشد إقناعا وأوقع عند الجمهور لمثلهم إلى الأمثلة. وإنهما أصلاً حجاج الجدل؛ ويتم ذلك بالمواضع، وتكتسب الملكة الجدلية بأدوات ثلاثة: [أ] أحدها أن يكون الجدلي قد اكتسب المشهورات وجمعها وحفظ ما يراه الجمهور كيف كان فإن من الذوائع ما هو مشهور جدا، ومنها ما هو قريب منه. ويمكن أن تكتسب الذوائع بالقريبة على ما يكون بينا؛ وذلك على سبيل المشابهة، أو على سبيل المقابلة. ويجب مع ذلك أن يقصد إلى الآراء الخاصة بصناعة صناعة مثل آراء بقراط في الطب، وفيثاغورس في الموسيقى
Chapter: On the instruments by which the dialectical skill is perfected and the seeking of topics (mawadi'), and they are four. Since the discussion has passed on the expansion of dialectical matters – which are the predicates, and what follows them which is that from which dialectical speech originates, namely premises, and that in which speech occurs, namely the sought propositions – it is necessary to consider the dialectical speech itself, which is the ultimate composition, namely the argument. So we say: every argument is either a syllogism or induction. The syllogism is closer to the intellect and more compelling, while induction is closer to sense perception and more persuasive, and it has more impact on the public due to their like for examples. And they are both the foundations of dialectical argumentation; and that is completed by the topics. And the dialectical skill is acquired through three tools: [A] One of them is that the dialectician should have acquired the well-known premises, collected them, and memorized what the public hold, however it may be. For among the stock of commonplaces, some are very well-known, and some are close to it. And it is possible to acquire the stock of commonplaces by closeness to what is evident, and that is by way of similarity or by way of opposition. And along with that, he must aim at the opinions specific to each craft, such as the opinions of Hippocrates in medicine and Pythagoras in music.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This is the opening of a new chapter (fasl) on the instruments (alat) that perfect the dialectical skill and the seeking of topics (mawadi'). The author first summarizes that earlier discussions covered predicates (mahmulat), premises (muqaddimat), and sought propositions (matalib). Now he turns to the argument (hujja) itself, which is the ultimate composition. He states that every argument is either a syllogism (qiyas) or induction (istiqra'). The syllogism is more intellectual and compelling, while induction is more sensory and persuasive, especially for the public. Both are foundations of dialectical argumentation, and they are completed by the topics (mawadi'). Then the author lists the tools for acquiring the dialectical skill. He says there are three tools, though the chapter heading said four. The first tool is that the dialectician must collect and memorize the well-known premises (mashhurat) and what the public holds. Among commonplaces (dhawa'i'), some are very well-known, others are close to well-known. One can acquire these commonplaces through similarity (tashbih) or opposition (muqabala). Additionally, the dialectician should know the specific opinions of each discipline, like Hippocrates' opinions in medicine and Pythagoras' in music. This shows that dialectical training requires both general common opinions and specialized knowledge. The example of Hippocrates and Pythagoras illustrates that the dialectician must be familiar with the authoritative views in various fields to argue effectively about matters related to them.

Key Terms
الحجة
القياس
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of argument is 'closer to the intellect and more compelling'?
A) Induction
B) Syllogism
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Every dialectical argument is either a syllogism or induction, with syllogism compelling the intellect and induction persuading through sense and examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a simple syllogism and a simple induction on the topic of 'the obligation of prayer' (wujub al-salat). Show how each would be used in a debate against someone who denies it.
?
Identify whether each of the following is a syllogism or induction: (a) 'All intoxicants are forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; so wine is forbidden.' (b) 'I have seen that most scholars agree that riba is forbidden; therefore, riba is forbidden.'

The First Tool: Acquiring Well-Known Premises
Author describes the first tool for acquiring dialectical skill

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of collecting and generating well-known premises (mashhurat and dhawa'i') through similarity and opposition for use in dialectical debates.

Core Idea
Dialectical skill is built by memorizing common opinions and generating new ones by analogy or contrast.

After establishing that dialectical arguments are built on syllogisms and inductions, the author now explains how a dialectician can acquire the raw material for these arguments: well-known premises (mashhurat) and commonplaces (dhawa'i'). The first tool is essentially a memory bank of public opinions, supplemented by techniques to generate related premises through similarity (tashbih) and opposition (muqabala). He also stresses the importance of knowing the authoritative opinions of specific disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first tool's goal
The author says the dialectician must 'acquire the well-known premises, collect them, and memorize what the public hold'. This is the first of three tools. Realize that this is a systematic endeavor, not casual knowledge.
The term 'mashhurat' refers to widely accepted opinions.
2
Examine two levels of commonplaces
The author distinguishes between commonplaces (dhawa'i') that are 'very well-known' and those 'close to it'. Identify examples from everyday life or Islamic tradition.
A very well-known commonplace: 'Justice is good'. A close one: 'Punishing the guilty is just'.
3
Learn the generative methods
The author tells us one can acquire commonplaces through similarity (tashbih) or opposition (muqabala). Apply this: if 'helping the needy is praiseworthy' is well-known, then by similarity 'helping the orphan is praiseworthy'; by opposition 'harming the needy is blameworthy'.
These methods help expand the repertoire.
4
Note the need for specialized knowledge
The author adds that the dialectician must also know the opinions specific to each field (like Hippocrates in medicine, Pythagoras in music). This shows that a mere collection of common opinions is insufficient; one must be well-versed in the authoritative views of relevant disciplines.
In Islamic debates, this would include opinions of leading jurists or theologians.

Source Text

Distinguishing al-Matlub and al-Wad' in Dialectic
Author defines key terms for dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-matlub and al-wad' in dialectic and explain how the same terms are used differently in the Book of Demonstration.

Core Idea
In dialectic, a position is any claim held by its holder, not a proven premise.

The author clarifies that what is called a position in dialectic differs from its usage in demonstration. This distinction is crucial for understanding how dialectical arguments start from accepted opinions rather than certain premises. This lesson will walk you through the author's definitions and apply them to a debate in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Find the definitions
Locate where the author defines al-matlub (المطلوب) and al-wad' (الوضع). Note that 'the demanded is not a position, but a position may become demanded'.
2
Compare with demonstrative usage
The author contrasts the dialectical wad' with the wad' in the Book of Demonstration: there, it is a claim not supported by proof. Here, it is any claim relative to its holder.
3
Apply to a kalam debate
In the debate on the world's eternity, the claim 'the world is temporally originated' is a wad' of the mutakallimun; the question 'Is the world eternal?' is the matlub.
4
Reflect on importance
Why does this matter? Dialectical syllogisms start from positions accepted by the opponent, not from certain premises. Confusing the two leads to equivocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهبوا إلى أن اللذة خير، والفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إليه، أو يكون فيه للفلاسفة فيما بينهم اختلاف أو يكون فيه للجمهور فيما بينهم اختلاف.
واعلم بأن المطلوب من حيث هو المطلوب لا يكون وضعا وإن كان الوضع قد يصير مطلوبًا بوجه ما كما إذا وقع فيه للأوائل خلاف والرأي أي رأي كان فإنه بالقياس إلى باصره وضع أي دعوى؛ وليس ما تسميته في كتاب البرهان وضعًا أعني الدعوى الذي لا يكون مؤيد الحجة بصحته. وإنما سمي ذلك وضعًا لمناسبة ما بينه وبين الوضع هنا؛ فإن من الدعاوي ما هو دعوى فقط، ومن الأوضاع ما هو وضع فقط.
فصل: في الآلات التي تتم بها ملكة الجدل وطلب المواضع وهي أربع ولما مر الكلام في بسط الأمور الجدلية وهي المحمولات، وما يليها وهو الذي منه الكلام الجدلي أعني المقدّمات، وما فيه الكلام أعني المطالب؛ فلا بد من النظر في نفس الكلام الجدلي وهو الذي إليه غاية التركيب وهو الحجة. فنقول كل حجة إما قياس وإما استقراء. والقياس أقرب إلى العقل وأشد إلزاما، والاستقراء أقرب إلى الحس وأشد إقناعا وأوقع عند الجمهور لمثلهم إلى الأمثلة. وإنهما أصلاً حجاج الجدل؛ ويتم ذلك بالمواضع، وتكتسب الملكة الجدلية بأدوات ثلاثة:
أ أحدها أن يكون الجدلي قد اكتسب المشهورات وجمعها وحفظ ما يراه الجمهور كيف كان فإن من الذوائع ما هو مشهور جدا، ومنها ما هو قريب منه. ويمكن أن تكتسب الذوائع بالقريبة على ما يكون بينا؛ وذلك على سبيل المشابهة، أو على سبيل المقابلة. ويجب مع ذلك أن يقصد إلى الآراء الخاصة بصناعة صناعة مثل آراء بقراط في الطب، وفيثاغورس في الموسيقى وغير ذلك فيجمعها جمعا. ويعلم أن المقدّمات والمسائل ثلاثة أصناف: ١ أحدها منطقية يراد لغيرها من الأمور النظرية والعملية، ٢ والثاني خُلقية وهو فيما إلينا أن نعلمه وهو المتعلق بالمطلوب والمهروب إما تعلقا أولا مثل قولنا هل أفعال العفّة سعادة، وإما تعلقاً ثانيا وهو أن يكون نفس المسألة كقولهم هل يمكن إزالة الخُلق . ٣ والثالث طبيعية مثل اعتقادنا أن النفس باقية وأنها يجب أن تفارق البدن مقدّمة ظاهرة؛ فإن هذا ينفع بوجه من الوجوه في العلم الخلقي ولكن لا بذاته. ولنرتب أمثلة للأصناف الثلاثة في موضوع واحد. فنقول : أما للمسألة المنطقية فقولنا هل المتضادات توجد حد بعضها في بعض، وأما الخلقية فقولنا هل اللذة مؤثرة أم لا؟ وأما الطبيعية فقولنا هل العالم أزلي أم لا، فيجب إذا تأمل المشهورات ليستنبطها، ويحصلها من المسموع والمكتوب أن يصف ما يقع في فن واحد بعضه إلى بعض، ويجمعه في مجموع واحد. فإن القياس الجدلي لا يكون إلا من مقدمات جدلية وهي المشهورات؛ ويجب أن يجتهد حتى تكون المقدمات مأخوذة عنده من وجهين أحدهما أن يصيرها مجموعةً إلى حكم عام وهذا للحفظ، والثاني أن تكون مفصَّلة عنده مقرّبة إلى الجزئيات حتى يكون له تصرف في كثيره؛ وهذا السبب أنفع للاستعمال كما مرَّ سالفا. فإذا كان عنده المتقابلات فصل ذلك إلى المتضادات وما معها، وإذا كان عنده المتضادات فصل ذلك إلى الأضداد الجزئية فيقال مثلا العلم بالحار والبارد واحد، والعلم بالرطب واليابس واحد، والعلم الفضيلة والرذيلة واحد.
ب وأما الآلة الثانية فهي القدرة على تفصيل الاسم المشترك والمتشابه والمتشكك؛ وهذا ليس قانوناً كالوضع أي بحيث يولد مقدمات بل إنما منفعته ما سَيَرِدُ بعد ذكره. ولا يجب أن يقتصر على أن يعلم أن لفظ كذا لفظ مشترك
They went to the view that pleasure is good, and the philosophers did not go to it, or there is among the philosophers a difference, or there is among the public a difference. And know that the demanded (matlub) inasmuch as it is demanded is not a position (wad'), though a position may become demanded in some respect, as when difference occurs among the early authorities, and opinion, any opinion whatsoever – it is, relative to its holder, a position, i.e., a claim; and it is not what you call 'position' in the Book of Demonstration, meaning the claim that is not supported by proof of its correctness. Rather, it is called 'position' because of an appropriateness between it and 'position' here; for among claims are those that are only claims, and among positions are those that are only positions. Chapter: On the instruments by which the faculty of dialectic is perfected and the seeking of topics (mawāḍiʿ); they are four. And since the discussion has passed on the exposition of dialectical matters – they are the predicates, and what follows them which is that from which dialectical discourse comes, namely premises, and that in which discourse occurs, namely demands – it is necessary to examine dialectical discourse itself, which is the ultimate composition, namely the argument (ḥujja). So we say: Every argument is either a syllogism (qiyās) or an induction (istiqrāʾ). The syllogism is closer to the intellect and more compelling, and the induction is closer to the senses and more persuasive, and more effective with the public for people like them due to examples. They are the two foundations of dialectical argument; that is completed by topics, and the dialectical faculty is acquired through three tools: (A) The first is that the dialectician should have acquired commonly accepted premises (mashhūrāt), collected them, and preserved what the public sees however it may be; for among commonly accepted things are those that are very famous, and among them are those near to it. It is possible to acquire the commonly accepted through what is near to it in a way that is evident; that is by way of similarity or by way of opposition. And along with that, he should aim at the opinions specific to each craft – such as the opinions of Hippocrates in medicine, and Pythagoras in music, and others – and collect them completely. And he knows that premises and issues are of three types: (1) The first is logical, intended for other theoretical and practical matters. (2) The second is ethical, concerning what is within our power to know, and it is related to what is sought and what is avoided – either primarily, like our saying 'Are the acts of chastity happiness?', or secondarily, where the issue itself is like their saying 'Is it possible to remove character?' (3) The third is physical, like our belief that the soul is permanent and that it must separate from the body as a manifest premise; for this is useful in some way in ethical science but not in itself. Let us arrange examples for the three types on a single subject. We say: As for the logical issue, it is our saying 'Do opposites exist as limits of one another?'; as for the ethical issue, it is our saying 'Is pleasure effective or not?'; as for the physical issue, it is our saying 'Is the world eternal or not?' Then, when he contemplates commonly accepted premises to derive them, and obtains them from what is heard and written, he must arrange what occurs in one craft part by part, and gather it into a single collection. For the dialectical syllogism is only from dialectical premises, which are the commonly accepted; and he must strive so that the premises are taken by him from two aspects: one is that he makes them grouped under a general judgment – this is for memorization; the second is that they be detailed with him, brought close to particulars so that he may have command over much of it – this aspect is more useful for application, as has passed earlier. So when he has the opposites, he divides them into contraries and what goes with them; when he has the contraries, he divides them into particular contraries, as one says: 'The knowledge of hot and cold is one, the knowledge of wet and dry is one, the knowledge of virtue and vice is one.' (B) As for the second tool, it is the ability to differentiate the common name (mushtarak), the ambiguous (mutashābih), and the equivocal (mutashakkik). This is not a rule like a position, i.e., such that it generates premises; rather, its benefit will come later after it is mentioned. And he should not limit himself to knowing that such-and-such a word is a common name.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the instruments of dialectic (jadal) and the seeking of topics (mawāḍiʿ). The author begins by clarifying the terms 'al-matlūb' (the demanded) and 'al-waḍʿ' (position), distinguishing the technical use in dialectic from its use in the Book of Demonstration (Burhān). In dialectic, a position is any claim relative to its holder, even if unsupported, whereas in demonstration it is an unproven claim. After this preliminary, the author announces the chapter: the instruments by which the dialectical faculty is perfected, stating there are four. However, the text then says 'three tools' – a slight inconsistency typical of rough drafts. The core of dialectical argument is the ḥujja (argument), which is either qiyās (syllogism) or istiqrāʾ (induction). Syllogism is more rational and compelling, induction is more sensory and persuasive, especially for the public. These two are the foundations of dialectical argumentation, and perfection comes through topics (mawāḍiʿ) and three tools. The first tool is to collect commonly accepted premises (mashhūrāt) and opinions from the public and from specific crafts (e.g., Hippocrates in medicine, Pythagoras in music). The author then categorises premises and issues into three types: logical (manṭiqiyya), ethical (khuluqiyya), and physical (ṭabīʿiyya). Examples are given: a logical issue asks whether opposites subsist in each other's limits; an ethical issue asks whether pleasure is effective; a physical issue asks whether the world is eternal. The dialectician must contemplate these and arrange them in a single collection. Then the author explains two aspects for handling premises: grouping under a general judgment for memorisation, and detailing with particular instances for practical application. The second tool is the ability to differentiate between homonymous (mushtarak), ambiguous (mutashābih), and equivocal (mutashakkik) names. This does not generate premises but aids in clarity; its full benefit will be explained later. The text breaks off mid-sentence at the page end.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in dialectic, what is 'al-waḍʿ' defined as?
A) A claim supported by proof
B) A claim unsupported by proof
C) A claim relative to its holder
D) A syllogism

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, a position is any claim held by a debater, not necessarily a proven premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a dialectical issue from usul al-fiqh, e.g., 'Is qiyas valid as a source of law?' Identify what would be the matlub and what would be a wad' in that debate.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
5 min
Syllogism and Induction in Dialectic
Author describes types of dialectical argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare syllogism (qiyas) and induction (istiqra) in terms of their proximity to intellect/senses and their persuasive effect.

Core Idea
Syllogism compels the intellect; induction persuades the senses and the public.

The author presents the two foundational types of argument in dialectic: syllogism and induction. Understanding their strengths is essential for choosing the right argument for a given audience. This lesson will help you identify and contrast these two forms based on the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hujja
Locate where the author says 'كل حجة إما قياس وإما استقراء' (every argument is either a syllogism or an induction).
2
Contrast their characteristics
Note: syllogism is 'أقرب إلى العقل وأشد إلزاما' (closer to intellect, more compelling). Induction is 'أقرب إلى الحس وأشد إقناعا' (closer to senses, more persuasive).
3
Identify audience effect
Induction is 'أوقع عند الجمهور' (more effective with the public) because it uses examples.
4
Apply to a fiqh ruling
For 'wine is forbidden', a syllogism uses 'every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'wine is an intoxicant'. Induction would list various intoxicants forbidden and infer the rule.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهبوا إلى أن اللذة خير، والفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إليه، أو يكون فيه للفلاسفة فيما بينهم اختلاف أو يكون فيه للجمهور فيما بينهم اختلاف.
واعلم بأن المطلوب من حيث هو المطلوب لا يكون وضعا وإن كان الوضع قد يصير مطلوبًا بوجه ما كما إذا وقع فيه للأوائل خلاف والرأي أي رأي كان فإنه بالقياس إلى باصره وضع أي دعوى؛ وليس ما تسميته في كتاب البرهان وضعًا أعني الدعوى الذي لا يكون مؤيد الحجة بصحته. وإنما سمي ذلك وضعًا لمناسبة ما بينه وبين الوضع هنا؛ فإن من الدعاوي ما هو دعوى فقط، ومن الأوضاع ما هو وضع فقط.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Collecting Commonly Accepted Premises (Mashhurat)
Author describes first tool of dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the three types of issues (logical, ethical, physical) and explain how a dialectician collects and organizes commonly accepted premises from various crafts.

Core Idea
Dialectical premises must be commonly accepted; they come from both universal opinions and craft-specific expert views.

The author explains that the first tool for acquiring dialectical faculty is to collect and organize mashhurat. These premises are the raw material for dialectical syllogisms. This lesson will guide you through the author's classification of issues and his method of grouping and detailing premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of issues
Find where the author lists: logical (manțiqiyya), ethical (khuluqiyya), physical (ţabī'iyya). Note the examples for each.
2
Understand the method of collection
The dialectician must gather mashhurat from the public and from specific crafts (e.g., Hippocrates in medicine, Pythagoras in music).
3
Grouping and detailing
The author advises grouping premises under general judgments for memorization and detailing them close to particulars for application.
4
Apply to a kalam debate
In a debate about the soul, a mashhura like 'the soul is permanent' (a physical issue) can be used as a premise in a dialectical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهبوا إلى أن اللذة خير، والفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إليه، أو يكون فيه للفلاسفة فيما بينهم اختلاف أو يكون فيه للجمهور فيما بينهم اختلاف.
واعلم بأن المطلوب من حيث هو المطلوب لا يكون وضعا وإن كان الوضع قد يصير مطلوبًا بوجه ما كما إذا وقع فيه للأوائل خلاف والرأي أي رأي كان فإنه بالقياس إلى باصره وضع أي دعوى؛ وليس ما تسميته في كتاب البرهان وضعًا أعني الدعوى الذي لا يكون مؤيد الحجة بصحته. وإنما سمي ذلك وضعًا لمناسبة ما بينه وبين الوضع هنا؛ فإن من الدعاوي ما هو دعوى فقط، ومن الأوضاع ما هو وضع فقط.
فصل: في الآلات التي تتم بها ملكة الجدل وطلب المواضع وهي أربع ولما مر الكلام في بسط الأمور الجدلية وهي المحمولات، وما يليها وهو الذي منه الكلام الجدلي أعني المقدّمات، وما فيه الكلام أعني المطالب؛ فلا بد من النظر في نفس الكلام الجدلي وهو الذي إليه غاية التركيب وهو الحجة. فنقول كل حجة إما قياس وإما استقراء. والقياس أقرب إلى العقل وأشد إلزاما، والاستقراء أقرب إلى الحس وأشد إقناعا وأوقع عند الجمهور لمثلهم إلى الأمثلة. وإنهما أصلاً حجاج الجدل؛ ويتم ذلك بالمواضع، وتكتسب الملكة الجدلية بأدوات ثلاثة:
أ أحدها أن يكون الجدلي قد اكتسب المشهورات وجمعها وحفظ ما يراه الجمهور كيف كان فإن من الذوائع ما هو مشهور جدا، ومنها ما هو قريب منه. ويمكن أن تكتسب الذوائع بالقريبة على ما يكون بينا؛ وذلك على سبيل المشابهة، أو على سبيل المقابلة. ويجب مع ذلك أن يقصد إلى الآراء الخاصة بصناعة صناعة مثل آراء بقراط في الطب، وفيثاغورس في الموسيقى وغير ذلك فيجمعها جمعا. ويعلم أن المقدّمات والمسائل ثلاثة أصناف: ١ أحدها منطقية يراد لغيرها من الأمور النظرية والعملية، ٢ والثاني خُلقية وهو فيما إلينا أن نعلمه وهو المتعلق بالمطلوب والمهروب إما تعلقا أولا مثل قولنا هل أفعال العفّة سعادة، وإما تعلقاً ثانيا وهو أن يكون نفس المسألة كقولهم هل يمكن إزالة الخُلق . ٣ والثالث طبيعية مثل اعتقادنا أن النفس باقية وأنها يجب أن تفارق البدن مقدّمة ظاهرة؛ فإن هذا ينفع بوجه من الوجوه في العلم الخلقي ولكن لا بذاته. ولنرتب أمثلة للأصناف الثلاثة في موضوع واحد. فنقول : أما للمسألة المنطقية فقولنا هل المتضادات توجد حد بعضها في بعض، وأما الخلقية فقولنا هل اللذة مؤثرة أم لا؟ وأما الطبيعية فقولنا هل العالم أزلي أم لا، فيجب إذا تأمل المشهورات ليستنبطها، ويحصلها من المسموع والمكتوب أن يصف ما يقع في فن واحد بعضه إلى بعض، ويجمعه في مجموع واحد. فإن القياس الجدلي لا يكون إلا من مقدمات جدلية وهي المشهورات؛ ويجب أن يجتهد حتى تكون المقدمات مأخوذة عنده من وجهين أحدهما أن يصيرها مجموعةً إلى حكم عام وهذا للحفظ، والثاني أن تكون مفصَّلة عنده مقرّبة إلى الجزئيات حتى يكون له تصرف في كثيره؛ وهذا السبب أنفع للاستعمال كما مرَّ سالفا. فإذا كان عنده المتقابلات فصل ذلك إلى المتضادات وما معها، وإذا كان عنده المتضادات فصل ذلك إلى الأضداد الجزئية فيقال مثلا العلم بالحار والبارد واحد، والعلم بالرطب واليابس واحد، والعلم الفضيلة والرذيلة واحد.
ب وأما الآلة الثانية فهي القدرة على تفصيل الاسم المشترك والمتشابه والمتشكك؛ وهذا ليس قانوناً كالوضع أي بحيث يولد مقدمات بل إنما منفعته ما سَيَرِدُ بعد ذكره. ولا يجب أن يقتصر على أن يعلم أن لفظ كذا لفظ مشترك
They went to the view that pleasure is good, and the philosophers did not go to it, or there is among the philosophers a difference, or there is among the public a difference. And know that the demanded (matlub) inasmuch as it is demanded is not a position (wad'), though a position may become demanded in some respect, as when difference occurs among the early authorities, and opinion, any opinion whatsoever – it is, relative to its holder, a position, i.e., a claim; and it is not what you call 'position' in the Book of Demonstration, meaning the claim that is not supported by proof of its correctness. Rather, it is called 'position' because of an appropriateness between it and 'position' here; for among claims are those that are only claims, and among positions are those that are only positions. Chapter: On the instruments by which the faculty of dialectic is perfected and the seeking of topics (mawāḍiʿ); they are four. And since the discussion has passed on the exposition of dialectical matters – they are the predicates, and what follows them which is that from which dialectical discourse comes, namely premises, and that in which discourse occurs, namely demands – it is necessary to examine dialectical discourse itself, which is the ultimate composition, namely the argument (ḥujja). So we say: Every argument is either a syllogism (qiyās) or an induction (istiqrāʾ). The syllogism is closer to the intellect and more compelling, and the induction is closer to the senses and more persuasive, and more effective with the public for people like them due to examples. They are the two foundations of dialectical argument; that is completed by topics, and the dialectical faculty is acquired through three tools: (A) The first is that the dialectician should have acquired commonly accepted premises (mashhūrāt), collected them, and preserved what the public sees however it may be; for among commonly accepted things are those that are very famous, and among them are those near to it. It is possible to acquire the commonly accepted through what is near to it in a way that is evident; that is by way of similarity or by way of opposition. And along with that, he should aim at the opinions specific to each craft – such as the opinions of Hippocrates in medicine, and Pythagoras in music, and others – and collect them completely. And he knows that premises and issues are of three types: (1) The first is logical, intended for other theoretical and practical matters. (2) The second is ethical, concerning what is within our power to know, and it is related to what is sought and what is avoided – either primarily, like our saying 'Are the acts of chastity happiness?', or secondarily, where the issue itself is like their saying 'Is it possible to remove character?' (3) The third is physical, like our belief that the soul is permanent and that it must separate from the body as a manifest premise; for this is useful in some way in ethical science but not in itself. Let us arrange examples for the three types on a single subject. We say: As for the logical issue, it is our saying 'Do opposites exist as limits of one another?'; as for the ethical issue, it is our saying 'Is pleasure effective or not?'; as for the physical issue, it is our saying 'Is the world eternal or not?' Then, when he contemplates commonly accepted premises to derive them, and obtains them from what is heard and written, he must arrange what occurs in one craft part by part, and gather it into a single collection. For the dialectical syllogism is only from dialectical premises, which are the commonly accepted; and he must strive so that the premises are taken by him from two aspects: one is that he makes them grouped under a general judgment – this is for memorization; the second is that they be detailed with him, brought close to particulars so that he may have command over much of it – this aspect is more useful for application, as has passed earlier. So when he has the opposites, he divides them into contraries and what goes with them; when he has the contraries, he divides them into particular contraries, as one says: 'The knowledge of hot and cold is one, the knowledge of wet and dry is one, the knowledge of virtue and vice is one.' (B) As for the second tool, it is the ability to differentiate the common name (mushtarak), the ambiguous (mutashābih), and the equivocal (mutashakkik). This is not a rule like a position, i.e., such that it generates premises; rather, its benefit will come later after it is mentioned. And he should not limit himself to knowing that such-and-such a word is a common name.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the instruments of dialectic (jadal) and the seeking of topics (mawāḍiʿ). The author begins by clarifying the terms 'al-matlūb' (the demanded) and 'al-waḍʿ' (position), distinguishing the technical use in dialectic from its use in the Book of Demonstration (Burhān). In dialectic, a position is any claim relative to its holder, even if unsupported, whereas in demonstration it is an unproven claim. After this preliminary, the author announces the chapter: the instruments by which the dialectical faculty is perfected, stating there are four. However, the text then says 'three tools' – a slight inconsistency typical of rough drafts. The core of dialectical argument is the ḥujja (argument), which is either qiyās (syllogism) or istiqrāʾ (induction). Syllogism is more rational and compelling, induction is more sensory and persuasive, especially for the public. These two are the foundations of dialectical argumentation, and perfection comes through topics (mawāḍiʿ) and three tools. The first tool is to collect commonly accepted premises (mashhūrāt) and opinions from the public and from specific crafts (e.g., Hippocrates in medicine, Pythagoras in music). The author then categorises premises and issues into three types: logical (manṭiqiyya), ethical (khuluqiyya), and physical (ṭabīʿiyya). Examples are given: a logical issue asks whether opposites subsist in each other's limits; an ethical issue asks whether pleasure is effective; a physical issue asks whether the world is eternal. The dialectician must contemplate these and arrange them in a single collection. Then the author explains two aspects for handling premises: grouping under a general judgment for memorisation, and detailing with particular instances for practical application. The second tool is the ability to differentiate between homonymous (mushtarak), ambiguous (mutashābih), and equivocal (mutashakkik) names. This does not generate premises but aids in clarity; its full benefit will be explained later. The text breaks off mid-sentence at the page end.

Key Takeaway
The dialectician must gather commonly accepted premises from both public opinion and expert crafts, organized by type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Collect one commonly accepted premise from each of the three types (logical, ethical, physical) that could be used in a debate about the permissibility of music. Use Islamic sources where possible.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Differentiating Homonyms for Dialectical Clarity
Author describes second tool of dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-mushtarak, al-mutashabih, and al-mutashakkik and explain why this tool is important for clarity in dialectic.

Core Idea
Words with multiple meanings can cause confusion; the dialectician must master their differentiation.

The second tool for dialectical faculty is the ability to differentiate common names (mushtarak), ambiguous (mutashabih), and equivocal (mutashakkik) terms. The author notes that this tool does not generate premises directly but prevents errors in argumentation. This lesson will help you recognize and apply this tool in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define the three terms
Mushtarak: same word, multiple meanings. Mutashabih: ambiguous in context. Mutashakkik: gradational, sharing a meaning in different degrees.
2
Understand its purpose
The author states this tool is not a rule that generates premises, but its benefit will come later (likely in avoiding equivocation).
3
Give Islamic examples
The word 'shari'a' can mean the revealed law or the path; 'imam' can mean leader or prayer leader; 'salah' is not homonymous, but 'din' can mean religion or judgment.
4
Apply to a debate
In a debate about obedience, if 'amr' is used ambiguously (command vs. affair), the argument could be fallacious unless the term is clarified.

Source Text

ذهبوا إلى أن اللذة خير، والفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إليه، أو يكون فيه للفلاسفة فيما بينهم اختلاف أو يكون فيه للجمهور فيما بينهم اختلاف.
واعلم بأن المطلوب من حيث هو المطلوب لا يكون وضعا وإن كان الوضع قد يصير مطلوبًا بوجه ما كما إذا وقع فيه للأوائل خلاف والرأي أي رأي كان فإنه بالقياس إلى باصره وضع أي دعوى؛ وليس ما تسميته في كتاب البرهان وضعًا أعني الدعوى الذي لا يكون مؤيد الحجة بصحته. وإنما سمي ذلك وضعًا لمناسبة ما بينه وبين الوضع هنا؛ فإن من الدعاوي ما هو دعوى فقط، ومن الأوضاع ما هو وضع فقط.
فصل: في الآلات التي تتم بها ملكة الجدل وطلب المواضع وهي أربع ولما مر الكلام في بسط الأمور الجدلية وهي المحمولات، وما يليها وهو الذي منه الكلام الجدلي أعني المقدّمات، وما فيه الكلام أعني المطالب؛ فلا بد من النظر في نفس الكلام الجدلي وهو الذي إليه غاية التركيب وهو الحجة. فنقول كل حجة إما قياس وإما استقراء. والقياس أقرب إلى العقل وأشد إلزاما، والاستقراء أقرب إلى الحس وأشد إقناعا وأوقع عند الجمهور لمثلهم إلى الأمثلة. وإنهما أصلاً حجاج الجدل؛ ويتم ذلك بالمواضع، وتكتسب الملكة الجدلية بأدوات ثلاثة:

They went to the view that pleasure is good, and the philosophers did not go to it, or there is among the philosophers a difference, or there is among the public a difference. And know that the demanded (matlub) inasmuch as it is demanded is not a position (wad'), though a position may become demanded in some respect, as when difference occurs among the early authorities, and opinion, any opinion whatsoever – it is, relative to its holder, a position, i.e., a claim; and it is not what you call 'position' in the Book of Demonstration, meaning the claim that is not supported by proof of its correctness. Rather, it is called 'position' because of an appropriateness between it and 'position' here; for among claims are those that are only claims, and among positions are those that are only positions. Chapter: On the instruments by which the faculty of dialectic is perfected and the seeking of topics (mawāḍiʿ); they are four. And since the discussion has passed on the exposition of dialectical matters – they are the predicates, and what follows them which is that from which dialectical discourse comes, namely premises, and that in which discourse occurs, namely demands – it is necessary to examine dialectical discourse itself, which is the ultimate composition, namely the argument (ḥujja). Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the instruments of dialectic (jadal) and the seeking of topics (mawāḍiʿ). The author begins by clarifying the terms 'al-matlūb' (the demanded) and 'al-waḍʿ' (position), distinguishing the technical use in dialectic from its use in the Book of Demonstration (Burhān). In dialectic, a position is any claim relative to its holder, even if unsupported, whereas in demonstration it is an unproven claim. After this preliminary, the author announces the chapter: the instruments by which the dialectical faculty is perfected, stating there are four. However, the text then says 'three tools' – a slight inconsistency typical of rough drafts. The core of dialectical argument is the ḥujja (argument), which is either qiyās (syllogism) or istiqrāʾ (induction). Syllogism is more rational and compelling, induction is more sensory and persuasive, especially for the public. These two are the foundations of dialectical argumentation, and perfection comes through topics (mawāḍiʿ) and three tools. The first tool is to collect commonly accepted premises (mashhūrāt) and opinions from the public and from specific crafts (e.g., Hippocrates in medicine, Pythagoras in music). The author then categorises premises and issues into three types: logical (manṭiqiyya), ethical (khuluqiyya), and physical (ṭabīʿiyya). Examples are given: a logical issue asks whether opposites subsist in each other's limits; an ethical issue asks whether pleasure is effective; a physical issue asks whether the world is eternal. The dialectician must contemplate these and arrange them in a single collection. Then the author explains two aspects for handling premises: grouping under a general judgment for memorisation, and detailing with particular instances for practical application. The second tool is the ability to differentiate between homonymous (mushtarak), ambiguous (mutashābih), and equivocal (mutashakkik) names. This does not generate premises but aids in clarity; its full benefit will be explained later. The text breaks off mid-sentence at the page end.

Using Logical Instruments to Avoid Equivocation
Explains benefit of logical instruments in preventing equivocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify equivocation in a theological debate and apply the logical instruments to clarify the subject of discussion.

Core Idea
Logical instruments prevent debates from becoming meaningless by ensuring both parties speak of the same thing.

This passage from the logical text explains the practical benefit of the four instruments: they force debaters to refine their research (taḥrīr al-baḥth) and avoid talking past each other. Without them, theological disputes on topics like God's visibility or the nature of the Qur'an can become exercises in mutual blindness. You will see how clarifying terms before arguing is a logical necessity, not just a courtesy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
In each example, find the term that is used in two different senses by the disputants. For the first example, it is 'jā'iz al-ru'ya' (visible); for the second, 'kalām Allāh' (speech of God).
2
Uncover the differing meanings
Determine what each debater actually means by the term. One means 'perceivable by sight in a certain manner' and the other denies that entirely. Similarly, one means the eternal attribute, the other means the created text.
3
Apply taḥrīr al-baḥth (refining research)
Use the logical instruments to pinpoint the exact meaning: define the term operationally before the debate proceeds. This aligns with the purpose of the instruments as highlighted in the passage.
The author says the instruments include an indication to refining research.
4
Assess the benefit
Recognise that without such refinement, the debate is not about the same subject – it is 'this on one side and that on another' (متساعيان على العمى). The instruments save the debate from futility.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منافع هذه الآلات فظاهرة لما أنها مشتملة على الإشارة إلى تحرير البحث وإلا لكان ما يقوله الموجب في البعض من الصور غير ما يسوق السائل كلامه إليه كما إذا قال واحد إنه تعالى جائز الرؤية، والآخر يقول إنه تعالى لا يكون جائز الرؤية يعني لا يمكن أن يكون مدركًا بالبصر إدراك شيء مقابل كذا وكذا؛ وذلك يسلم هذا إذا صرَّح به لكنه يقول إن جائز ويعني به معنى لا يقدر على النطق به ثمَّ يتساعيان على العمى. وكذلك إذا قال أحدهما كلام الله تعالی غیر مخلوق وعنى به المسموع، وقال الآخر به مخلوق وعنى به شيئًا آخر فكلامهما لا يكون في محل واحد بل هذا في جانب، وذلك في جانب
As for the benefits of these instruments, they are apparent because they include an indication to refining research; otherwise what the proponent says in some forms would not be what the questioner directs his speech to, as when one says 'God is visible (jā'iz al-ru'ya)' and the other says 'God is not visible' meaning He cannot be perceived by sight perceiving something opposite such and such. This is fine if he explicitly states it, but he says 'visible' and means a meaning he cannot articulate, then they both grope in blindness. Similarly, if one says 'The speech of God is uncreated' meaning the audible, and the other says 'It is created' meaning something else, then their words are not about the same subject, but this is on one side and that on another.

The Fourth Tool: Grasping Similarities for Induction
Author defines the fourth tool and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the fourth logical tool (taking similarities) and identify its role in induction.

Core Idea
Finding likeness across distant genera fuels induction.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth logical tool as the ability to grasp similarities between things that are far apart in definition and genus. He gives examples like the similarity between substance and quantity in having no opposite, and relational proportions like sense:sensible as knowledge:known. This tool is the foundation of induction (istiqra'), which seeks commonalities to establish universal predications. We'll explore how this works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth tool's function
The passage states that the fourth tool is 'the ability to take similarities' (الاقتدار على أخذ المتشابهات). Determine what kind of similarities are meant: not obvious ones but those between things of distant genera.
2
Recognize its domain: distant genera
The author specifies that this tool is for things 'of distant genera and different definitions' (الأشياء البعيدة الأجناس المختلفة حدا). Contrast this with homogeneous things that are similar by nature.
3
Observe the two types of similarity
The passage gives examples: a single shared predicate (like substance and quantity both having no opposite) and relational proportion (like the ratio of sense to sensible being like knowledge to known). Identify these patterns.
Both types involve things that are not obviously alike but share a deeper structural similarity.
4
Connect to induction
The author says the fourth tool is useful in induction because induction is based on 'seeking similar things according to a universal and another universal' to predicate one universal of another. See how finding similarities across diverse instances allows a general rule to be formed.
In usul al-fiqh, induction is used to derive principles from multiple cases, e.g., the prohibition of intoxicants from wine and nabidh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحكام العلم. وأما الأشياء المتباعدة حدا المختلفة الأجناس التي لا تشابه فيه في طبائعها ولا في أجناسها ولا موضوعاتها. فإن اعتبار الفرق بينها كأنه حوض في تعرف أمر معلوم؛ وذلك غير مقتدر به. وأما الآلة الرابعة فهي الاقتدار على أخذ المتشابهات؛ ويجب أن يكون ذلك متطلبا في الأشياء البعيدة الأجناس المختلفة حدا. فمن ذلك يكون معنى مفرد مثل تشابه الجوهر والكم في أنهما من جملة ما لا ضد له ومنه ما يكون بالنسبة كما يقال إن نسبة الحس إلى المحسوس هي نسبة العلم إلى المعلوم أو نسبة البياض إلى البصر كنسبة السواد إليه، ونسبة العقل إلى الخيال كنسبة الخيال إلى الحس. وبالجملة إما أن لا يكون في الحدود مشترك، أو يكون مشترك واحد هو في كليهما منسوب أو منسوب إليه، أو في أحدهما منسوب وفي الآخر منسوب إليه، ويجب أن يكون هذا كله في الأشياء المتباعدة. وأيضًا فإن الأشياء المتجانسة إذا كانت بعد التجانس متباينة حدا كالإنسان والفرس وكالبياض والسواد، فإن ارتياد ما يجمعهما بعد التجانس نافع في الدربة. وأما منافع هذه الآلات فظاهرة لما أنها مشتملة على الإشارة إلى تحرير البحث وإلا لكان ما يقوله الموجب في البعض من الصور غير ما يسوق السائل كلامه إليه كما إذا قال واحد إنه تعالى جائز الرؤية، والآخر يقول إنه تعالى لا يكون جائز الرؤية يعني لا يمكن أن يكون مدركًا بالبصر إدراك شيء مقابل كذا وكذا؛ وذلك يسلم هذا إذا صرَّح به لكنه يقول إن جائز ويعني به معنى لا يقدر على النطق به ثمَّ يتساعيان على العمى. وكذلك إذا قال أحدهما كلام الله تعالی غیر مخلوق وعنى به المسموع، وقال الآخر به مخلوق وعنى به شيئًا آخر فكلامهما لا يكون في محل واحد بل هذا في جانب، وذلك في جانب آخر. وأما الآلة الثانية وهي الاقتدار على تفصيل اسم مشترك فإنه يُعِينُ في هذا الباب معونة عظيمة فإنه إذا قال أحدهما العين كذا، وقال الآخر العين ليس كذا؛ فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون بينهما خلاف بل يمكن أن يغالط هذا ذلك؛ وذلك من جملة ما يجب التحرُّز عنه، وسيجيء من بعد مثل هذه المغالطات. وأما الثالثة وهي المبنية على طلب الفصول فمنفعتها في ارتياد المواضع والمقدمات المعدَّة نحو القياسات. وفي الحدود كذلك فإن كمال الحدود بالفصول. وأما الرابعة المبنية على طلب التشابه فهي نافعة في الاستقراء؛ إذ الاستقراء مبني على طلب أمور متشابهة بحسب كلي وكلي آخر لجعل أحد الكليتين محمول على الآخر.
And the rulings of knowledge. As for things that are distant in definition, of different genera, having no similarity in their natures, genera, or subjects, then considering the difference between them is like a basin in knowing a known matter; and that is not to be attempted. And as for the fourth tool, it is the ability to take similarities; and that must be sought in things of distant genera and different definitions. From that is a single meaning like the similarity of substance and quantity in that they are among things that have no opposite, and from it is what is by relation, as it is said that the ratio of sense to the sensible is the ratio of knowledge to the known, or the ratio of whiteness to sight is like the ratio of blackness to it, and the ratio of intellect to imagination is like the ratio of imagination to sense. In summary, either there is no common term in the definitions, or there is one common term that is in both of them related or related to, or in one related and in the other related to, and all of this must be in distant things. Also, homogeneous things when after homogeneity they are different in definition, like man and horse, and whiteness and blackness, then seeking what unites them after homogeneity is useful in practice. And as for the benefits of these tools, they are apparent because they include indication to refining the investigation; otherwise, what the affirmer says in some forms would not be what the questioner intends his speech towards, as when one says that He, exalted, is permissible to see, and the other says that He, exalted, is not permissible to see, meaning that He cannot be perceived by sight as perceiving something facing thus and such; and that is conceded if he states it explicitly, but he says 'permissible' and means by it a meaning he cannot express, then they go astray blindly. And likewise, if one says the speech of God, exalted, is uncreated and means the heard, and the other says it is created and means something else, then their speech is not in one place but this is in a side and that in another side. And as for the second tool, which is the ability to detail a homonym, it helps greatly in this matter, for if one says 'the eye is such' and the other says 'the eye is not such', then it is not necessary that there be a dispute between them, but rather one may deceive the other; and that is among the things that must be guarded against, and such fallacies will come later. And as for the third, which is based on seeking differentiae, its benefit lies in seeking the places and premises prepared for syllogisms, and likewise in definitions, for the perfection of definitions is by differentiae. And as for the fourth, based on seeking similarity, it is useful in induction, for induction is based on seeking similar things according to a universal and another universal to make one of the two universals predicated on the other.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the four logical tools (آلات) that are used in dialectical debate. The author begins by describing the fourth tool: the ability to grasp similarities between things that are far apart in definition and genus. He gives examples: substance and quantity share the property of having no opposite; analogical relations like the ratio of sense to the sensible being like knowledge to the known. The benefit of this tool is that it helps in induction, where one seeks similarities to establish universal predication. The author then discusses the benefits of all four tools together, emphasizing that they help refine investigation and prevent misunderstandings in debate. He illustrates with examples from kalam: one debater may say 'God is visible' meaning one thing, another denies it meaning another; or one says 'the Quran is uncreated' meaning the heard speech, another says 'created' meaning something else—without the tools, they talk past each other. The second tool, resolving homonyms (like 'eye'), prevents fallacies by clarifying ambiguous terms. The third tool, seeking differentiae, is useful for finding premises for syllogisms and perfecting definitions. The fourth tool aids induction. The overall message is that these tools are indispensable for precise dialectical engagement.

مشترك
فصل
استقراء

The Four Tools as Guardians of Dialectical Debate
Author explains benefits of all four tools for avoiding ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how each of the four logical tools prevents fallacies in dialectical debate, using examples from kalam.

Core Idea
Without the tools, debaters talk past each other; with them, fallacies dissolve.

The author then shifts to the benefits of all four tools, emphasizing that they refine investigation so that disputants do not misunderstand each other. He gives concrete examples from kalam: the dispute over God's visibility (jā'iz al-ru'ya) and the createdness of the Quran. He also shows how the second tool (detailing homonyms) prevents fallacies like the example of 'eye'. We'll examine these benefits.

Guided Path
1
List the four tools from the passage
The passage mentions four tools: the first is implied (general refining of investigation?), the second is detailing homonyms, the third is seeking differentiae, and the fourth is seeking similarities. Identify each from the description.
The first tool is not explicitly named but is referred to as 'الإشارة إلى تحرير البحث' (refining investigation).
2
Identify each tool's benefit
For each tool, find the stated benefit: second tool prevents fallacies from homonyms, third perfects definitions and finds premises, fourth aids induction. Write them down.
3
Analyze the kalam example: God's visibility
The passage gives a scenario: one says God is jā'iz al-ru'ya (permissible to see), another says not jā'iz (not possible). The intended meanings differ, causing confusion. Identify which tool addresses this.
The second tool (detailing homonyms) would clarify the term 'jā'iz' which can mean 'possible' or 'permissible'.
4
Analyze the Quran example
One says the Quran is uncreated (meaning the eternal speech), another says created (meaning the ink and paper). They are not in conflict if they specify their meanings. Which tool helps?
Again, the second tool resolves ambiguity, but also the first tool (refining investigation) ensures the topic is clear.
5
Use the second tool on the 'eye' example
The author says if one says 'the eye is such' and another says 'it is not such', they may be using 'eye' with different meanings (e.g., the organ vs. the spring). This is a fallacy of equivocation. The second tool requires that the term be detailed to avoid deception.
Recognize this as a classic fallacy; the tool prevents it by demanding specification.

C) The example of the word 'eye' meaning different things.
D) The ratio of sense to the sensible.

Key Takeaway
The four tools ensure that dialectical debate is about substance, not verbal confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'the Quran is makhluq' (created). One scholar means the ink and paper, another means the eternal speech of God. Use the second tool to show how this dispute can be resolved without contradiction.

Three Dialectical Tools for Sound Disputation
Author explains benefits of three tools: analyzing shared terms, seeking differentiate, and seeking similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three dialectical tools (analyzing shared terms, seeking differentiate, seeking similarity) and explain how each contributes to sound disputation.

Core Idea
Disputation is not rhetorical combat—it is a methodical application of logical tools.

In this passage, the author of a classical logic text enumerates three specific tools essential for rigorous disputation (jadal). These tools—analyzing shared terms, seeking differentiate, and seeking similarity—are not mere techniques for winning debates; they are logical instruments that prevent fallacies, perfect definitions, and enable inductive reasoning. Understanding each tool's distinct function equips you to engage in kalam and fiqh debates with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second tool
The second tool is the ability to analyze a shared term (ism mushtarak). This tool guards against the fallacy of equivocation: if two disputants use the same word with different meanings, they may falsely think they disagree. By detailing the term's multiple meanings, you can avoid spurious conflict.
The passage warns that such equivocation is a source of fallacy.
2
Connect the third tool to definitions and syllogisms
The third tool is seeking differentiate (fusooh). It helps in two ways: (a) completing definitions, since a definition is perfected by its differentia, and (b) finding premises for syllogisms. By isolating what distinguishes a thing, you can build accurate premises.
The author says the perfection of definitions is through differentiate.
3
Link the fourth tool to induction
The fourth tool is seeking similarity (tashabuh). This is the foundation of induction (istiqra'). Induction gathers similar particular instances under a universal, then predicates another universal. In Islamic legal reasoning, this supports extending rulings via qiyas.
Induction here is not complete enumeration but reasoning from shared attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآلة الثانية وهي الاقتدار على تفصيل اسم مشترك فإنه يُعِينُ في هذا الباب معونة عظيمة فإنه إذا قال أحدهما العين كذا، وقال الآخر العين ليس [۳۲۸] كذا؛ فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون بينهما خلاف بل يمكن أن يغالط هذا ذلك؛ وذلك من جملة ما يجب التحرُّر [؟] عنه، وسيجيء من بعد مثل هذه المغالطات. وأما الثالثة وهي المبنية على طلب الفصول فمنفعتها في ارتياد المواضع والمقدمات المعدَّة نحو القياسات. وفي الحدود كذلك فإن كمال الحدود بالفصول. وأما الرابعة المبنية على طلب التشابه فهي نافعة في الاستقراء؛ إذ الاستقراء مبني على طلب أمور متشابهة بحسب كلي وكلي آخر لجعل أحد الكليتين محمول على الآخر.
As for the second tool, which is the ability to detail a shared term, it is of great help in this chapter. For if one says 'the eye is such' and the other says 'the eye is not such', it does not necessarily entail disagreement between them, but rather one can deceive the other. That is among the things that must be guarded against, and similar fallacies will come later. As for the third tool, which is based on seeking the differentiate, its benefit lies in seeking out the places and premises prepared for syllogisms. Likewise in definitions, for the perfection of definitions is through differentiate. As for the fourth tool, based on seeking similarity, it is useful in induction; for induction is based on seeking similar things according to one universal and another universal, in order to make one of the two universals predicated on the other.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage from a larger work on logic discusses three specific tools used in disputation (jadal). The author describes the second tool as the ability to analyze a term that is shared or equivocal (اسم مشترك). By showing that a term can have multiple meanings, disputants can avoid false disagreements; one may think they are contradicting each other when they are actually speaking of different things. The author warns that such equivocation is a source of fallacy. The third tool involves seeking the differentiate (فصول) of things. This is beneficial for constructing definitions, since definitions are completed by their differentiae, and also for finding appropriate premises for syllogisms. The fourth tool is based on seeking similarity (تشابه), which is the foundation of induction (استقراء). Induction proceeds by gathering similar instances under a universal to establish another universal predication. The author thus shows how each tool has a distinct logical function.

Key Terms
الآلة الثانية
الآلة الثالثة
الآلة الرابعة
اسم مشترك
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary benefit of seeking differentiate (the third tool)?
A) To avoid fallacies from shared terms.
B) To find premises for syllogisms and perfect definitions.
C) To gather similar instances for induction.
D) To refute opposing views.

Key Takeaway
Each of the three dialectical tools serves a distinct logical purpose: analyzing shared terms prevents equivocation, seeking differentiate perfects definitions and supplies syllogistic premises, and seeking similarity enables induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a legal ruling: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. This substance is an intoxicant, so it is forbidden.' Identify which of the three tools would be most relevant to examine the term 'intoxicant' and explain why.
?
Given two cases of drinks that cause intoxication, use the fourth tool (seeking similarity) to construct an inductive argument that all such drinks are forbidden.

Following Common Usage in Naming
Author explains the principle of naming according to common usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct and indirect naming and explain why it is obligatory to follow common usage in naming meanings.

Core Idea
Names must follow common usage; don't invent your own.

In dialectical argument, you cannot arbitrarily assign names to meanings. The author establishes that one must follow the common people (al-jumhūr) in naming. He distinguishes direct naming (like calling animal 'animal') and indirect naming (calling a particular body 'animal' because it falls under the meaning). This rule is crucial for clear communication and avoiding sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of naming
Find where the author distinguishes direct naming (tasmīya bi-ghayr wāsiṭa) from indirect naming (tasmīya bi-wāsiṭa). Direct naming applies the name to the meaning itself; indirect applies it to something that falls under that meaning.
The example given: calling the meaning of animal 'animal' (direct) vs. calling a particular body 'animal' (indirect).
2
Recognize the obligation to follow common usage
Look for the statement that it is obligatory to follow the common people (al-jumhūr) in naming. The author says this is necessary because common usage determines what a word conventionally means.
The text: 'والواجب علينا اتباع الجمهور في التسمية للمعاني'.
3
Apply the rule to a dialectical context
Consider why inventing names would be problematic: if a speaker uses a name in a non-customary way, the argument becomes unclear and prone to sophistry. The dialectician must adhere to common usage to be understood.
If a word is suitable for a meaning in customary usage, it must be accepted; otherwise, confusion arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن من هذا القبيل أيضًا موضع بعد نحو اعتبار اللفظ ونافع في الإثبات والإبطال وهو أنه إن كان لم يأتِ للمعنى بالاسم المشهور له بل اخترع من نفسه اسما. أما على سبيل اشتراك فيه إذا كان لذلك الاسم عند الجمهور معنى آخر ؛ والواجب علينا اتباع الجمهور في التسمية للمعاني والتسمية على وجهين تسمية بغير واسطة كتسمية معنى الحيوان حيوانًا، وتسمية بواسطة كتسمية الجسم الفلاني حيوانا؛ وذلك بأن يجعل الشيء داخلا تحت المعنى الذي له الاسم أولا، وليس هذا أي الجمهور بل الجمهور لم يلتفتوا إلى ما لا يكون دالا على المعنى في المتعارف. فإذا ثبت أن هذا اللفظ صالح المتعارف لهذا المعنى فحينئذ يجب أن يُلتفت إليه ؛ وهذا الموضع جدلي أيضًا. وموضع آخر يمكن أن يكون جدليا، ويمكن أن يكون مغالطيّا. وذلك لأن الجدلي إذا استعمل لفظاً مشتركا ثم حكم عليه كلي لم يتبين استمراره في جميع تلك المعاني. وإنما يمكنه ذلك إذا كان المخاطب لا يشعر باشتراك الاسم؛ وهذا الموضع صالح للإثبات والإبطال. وأما المبطل فيكفيه أن يتبين في بعض تلك المعاني أن الحكم غير موجود إذا كان يبطل كليا. وأما المثبت فيحتاج إلى أن يثبت في الكل ثمَّ من الجزئي ما يكون في حكم الكلي حتى يثبت الكلي بإثبات الحكم في الجزئي كما ينتفي بنفي الحكم عن الجزئي؛ وذلك فيما يكون الخلاف في الكل والبعض واحدًا مثلا إذا قيل كل نفس إنسانية لا تموت فإنه في الخلاف مع قولنا بعض النفس الإنسانية لا تموت واحدا، فلذلك حكم ما يكون جزئياً ليس جزئياً في المعنى بل في اللفظ.
And know that of this kind also is a topic concerning the consideration of wording, useful in affirmation and refutation: namely, when one does not bring the well-known name for the meaning but invents a name himself, especially when there is homonymy, when that name has another meaning among the common people. And it is obligatory upon us to follow the common people in naming meanings. And naming is of two types: naming without an intermediary, like naming the meaning of animal 'animal', and naming with an intermediary, like naming a particular body 'animal'. That is by making the thing fall under the meaning that has the name first, and this is not the common people, rather the common people do not pay attention to what does not indicate the meaning in customary usage. So if it is established that this word is suitable in customary usage for this meaning, then it must be paid attention to. And this topic is also dialectical. And there is another topic that can be dialectical and can be sophistical. That is because the dialectician, if he uses a homonymous term and then makes a universal judgment, he does not make clear its continuation in all those meanings. He can only do that if the interlocutor is unaware of the homonymy of the name. And this topic is suitable for affirmation and refutation. As for the one who refutes, it suffices for him to show in some of those meanings that the judgment does not exist if he is refuting universally. As for the one who affirms, he needs to affirm it in all, then from the particular what is in the judgment of the universal, so that he affirms the universal by affirming the judgment in the particular, just as it is negated by negating the judgment from the particular. And that is in cases where the disagreement in the universal and the part is one, for example if it is said 'Every human soul does not die', then it is the same as the disagreement with our statement 'Some human souls do not die'. Therefore, the judgment that is particular is not particular in meaning but in wording.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage addresses the role of naming and common usage in logical argumentation, particularly in dialectical contexts. The author states that one should follow the common people in naming meanings, and distinguishes between direct naming (e.g., calling the meaning of 'animal' by the word 'animal') and indirect naming (e.g., calling a particular body 'animal' because it falls under the meaning). If a word is suitable for a meaning in customary usage, it must be accepted. This is a dialectical topic. Then a second topic is introduced: using homonymous terms. A dialectician might use a homonym and make a universal judgment without clarifying that the judgment holds for all meanings. He can only deceive if the interlocutor is unaware of the homonymy. For refutation, it suffices to show that the judgment does not hold for some meaning; for proof, one must show it holds for all. The author gives an example: 'Every human soul does not die' is logically equivalent to 'Some human souls do not die' when the disagreement is about the same thing (the universal vs. particular), but the difference is only verbal. This shows how homonyms can cause confusion.

Key Terms
الجمهور
تسمية بغير واسطة
تسمية بواسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the obligation regarding naming meanings?
A) One may invent new names arbitrarily.
B) One must follow the common people in naming.
C) One must use only direct naming.
D) One must avoid all homonymous terms.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical argument, naming must follow common usage to ensure clear communication and avoid sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from fiqh where a term has a common meaning and an indirect application (e.g., 'ṣalāh' originally means prayer but can be applied to specific acts). Explain which is direct and which is indirect naming.
