Using Ambiguous Terms in Dialectic and Sophistry
Author analyzes the use of homonymous terms in argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a homonymous term is used dialectically vs sophistically and evaluate the logical equivalence of universal and particular statements in a dispute.

Core Idea
Homonyms can trick or teach; know the difference.

The author discusses a second topic: using homonymous terms (lafẓ mushtarak). A dialectician can use a homonym to make a universal judgment, but only effectively if the interlocutor is unaware of the homonymy. This can be used for both affirmation and refutation. The author illustrates with an example about the human soul, showing that sometimes a universal and a particular statement are logically equivalent in dispute. This topic straddles dialectic and sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym
Find a word with multiple meanings in the argument. The author uses the example of a term that has different meanings among the common people.
In the example, the term 'soul' (nafs) might have different meanings, but here it is treated as univocal for the purpose of the argument.
2
Recognize the dialectical use
The dialectician makes a universal judgment without clarifying which meaning is intended, and the interlocutor is unaware of the ambiguity. This allows the dialectician to advance the argument.
The text: 'وإنما يمكنه ذلك إذا كان المخاطب لا يشعر باشتراك الاسم'.
3
Contrast with sophistical use
If the interlocutor were aware of the homonymy, the argument would fail and be seen as a sophism. The same move can be dialectical or sophistical depending on the opponent's awareness.
The author says this topic 'يمكن أن يكون جدليا، ويمكن أن يكون مغالطيّا'.
4
Analyze the example of the human soul
Consider the statement 'Every human soul does not die' (universal) and 'Some human souls do not die' (particular). In a dispute about whether the soul dies, these are logically equivalent because the disagreement is about the existence of death for any soul.
The author says: 'فلذلك حكم ما يكون جزئياً ليس جزئياً في المعنى بل في اللفظ'.

Source Text

The Obligation to Follow Common Usage in Naming
Dialectical place concerning word consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dialectician violates the obligation to follow common usage in naming and explain why it matters.

Core Idea
Dialectical argument relies on common linguistic convention, not invented terminology.

In dialectical argument, the meaning of words is crucial. The author introduces a 'place' concerning word consideration: if a dialectician uses a term not commonly known for a meaning, or invents a name, he must follow the common people's usage. This lesson unpacks that obligation and its implications for proof and refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under dispute
Find the term the dialectician uses that may not be the well-known name for the intended meaning.
Example: if someone calls a horse a 'quadruped' when 'horse' is the common name.
2
Determine if the term is commonly used for that meaning
Check if the term is conventionally understood by the common people (الجمهور) to indicate that meaning.
The passage says: 'the common people do not pay attention to what does not indicate the meaning in conventional usage.'
3
Check for equivocation (ishtirāk)
If the term has multiple common meanings, the dialectician must be clear which one he intends.
The author mentions using an invented name 'on the basis of equivocation' as a possible fault.
4
Apply the obligation to follow common usage
If the term is not conventionally suitable, the dialectician should use the well-known name or clarify. The rule: 'it is obligatory upon us to follow the common people in naming meanings.'
This prevents misunderstandings and ensures fair argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اتباع الجمهور في التسمية للمعاني والتسمية على وجهين تسمية بغير واسطة كتسمية معنى الحيوان حيوانًا، وتسمية بواسطة كتسمية الجسم الفلاني حيوانا؛ وذلك بأن يجعل الشيء داخلا تحت المعنى الذي له الاسم أولا، وليس هذا أي الجمهور بل الجمهور لم يلتفتوا إلى ما لا يكون دالا على المعنى في المتعارف. فإذا ثبت أن هذا اللفظ صالح المتعارف لهذا المعنى فحينئذ يجب أن يُلتفت إليه ؛ وهذا الموضع جدلي أيضًا. وموضع آخر يمكن أن يكون جدليا، ويمكن أن يكون مغالطيّا. وذلك لأن الجدلي إذا استعمل لفظاً مشتركا ثم حكم عليه كلي لم يتبين استمراره في جميع تلك المعاني. وإنما يمكنه ذلك إذا كان المخاطب لا يشعر باشتراك الاسم؛ وهذا الموضع صالح للإثبات والإبطال. وأما المبطل فيكفيه أن يتبين في بعض تلك المعاني أن الحكم غير موجود إذا كان يبطل كليا. وأما المثبت فيحتاج إلى أن يثبت في الكل ثمَّ من الجزئي ما يكون في حكم الكلي حتى يثبت الكلي بإثبات الحكم في الجزئي كما ينتفي بنفي الحكم عن الجزئي؛ وذلك فيما يكون الخلاف في الكل والبعض واحدًا مثلا إذا قيل كل نفس إنسانية لا تموت فإنه في الخلاف مع قولنا بعض النفس الإنسانية لا تموت واحدا، فلذلك حكم ما يكون جزئياً ليس جزئياً في المعنى بل في اللفظ. وبالجملة فالمحمول قد يكون بالاشتراك، وقد لا يكون بل يكون بالتواطؤ كما مر ، أو بالتشكيك كالموجود على الأشياء المختلفة في الحقيقة؛ وحينئذ يمكن أن يقع على البعض منها أولا وعلى البعض ثانيا، وقد يكون يوقع من التشكيك مختلطا كما أن اللفظ يدل على النسبة؛ والنسبة ليست كلها إلى غاية أو إلى مبدأ بل مثل العلم بالشيء غاية تعم ما يكون علما بما هو له كالمبدأ نحو العلم بالصحة وبما له الغاية / نحو العلم بالمتضادات. فيكون العلم [٣٢٩ ] بالشيء يعم كل واحد منهما عموم التشكيك ، وقد يكون بالذات وبالعرض أيضًا كالشهوة للشيء، فإن لفظة تكون على أنها غاية كالصحة، وعلى أنها مبدأ كالمداواة، وعلى ما بالذات كمن يشتهي الحلو لأنه حلو، وعلى ما بعرض كمن يشتهي الشراب لا لأنه شراب بل لأنه حلو؛ فيقال لجميع هذا مشتهى في التشكيك. ثم من المواضع ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدلي والبرهان، ومنها ما لا يكون مشتركا كما مر ، والموضع إما موضع من موضوعات الموضوع، أو موضع مما يقال على المحمول قولا، أو موضع مما يقال عليه المحمول قولا. وإن اتفق أن يتشارك بعض هذه المواضع في الموضوع فالاعتبار مختلف؛ وقد علمت أنه كيف يصلح ما سلف للإثبات والإبطال فلا حاجة إلى البيان.
It can be fixed, and it can not be. And if the questioner presents a syllogism in response to what the answerer posits as a syllogism, that is not an invalidation of the thing in itself, but rather a proof that entails invalidation. And know that of this kind also is a place concerning consideration of the word, useful in proof and refutation: that if he did not bring for the meaning its well-known name but invented a name for himself, either on the basis of equivocation if that name has another meaning among the common people; and it is obligatory upon us to follow the common people in naming meanings. And naming is of two types: naming without mediation, like naming the meaning of 'animal' as animal, and naming with mediation, like naming a particular body as animal, by making the thing fall under the meaning that has the name first. And this is not the common people, rather the common people do not pay attention to what does not indicate the meaning in conventional usage. So if it is established that this word is conventionally suitable for this meaning, then it must be attended to. And this place is also dialectical. Another place can be dialectical and can be sophistical. That is because the dialectician, if he uses an equivocal word and then makes a universal judgment, it does not become clear that it applies continuously to all those meanings. He can only do that if the interlocutor is unaware of the equivocation of the name; this place is suitable for proof and refutation. As for the refuter, it suffices for him to show in some of those meanings that the judgment does not exist if he is refuting a universal. As for the prover, he needs to establish in all, then from the particular what is in the status of the universal, so that the universal is established by establishing the judgment in the particular just as it is negated by negating the judgment from the particular. That is in cases where the dispute concerning the universal and the particular is one, for example if it is said 'Every human soul does not die', then in dispute with our saying 'Some human souls do not die' it is one; therefore the judgment of what is particular is not particular in meaning but in wording. In summary, the predicate may be by equivocation, or may not be but by univocity as previously, or by analogy like 'existent' applied to things different in reality; and then it can be applied to some of them first and to others second, and it may be a mixed analogy just as the word indicates a relation; and the relation is not all to a purpose or to a principle, but like knowledge of a thing as a purpose that includes what is knowledge of what is for it as a principle, like knowledge of health and knowledge of opposites. So knowledge of a thing includes each of them by analogical generality, and it may be essential and accidental as well, like desire for a thing: the word can be as a purpose like health, as a principle like medication, as essential like one who desires sweetness because it is sweet, and as accidental like one who desires drink not because it is drink but because it is sweet; so all of this is called 'desired' by analogy. Then among the places are those shared between the dialectical and the demonstrative, and among them are those not shared as previously. The place is either a place from the subjects of the subject, or a place from what is said of the predicate, or a place from what the predicate is said of. And if it happens that some of these places share in the subject, the consideration is different; and you have known how what preceded is suitable for proof and refutation, so there is no need for clarification.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on dialectical places (مواضع جدلية) focused on the role of word usage in proof and refutation. It begins by noting that a place can be either fixed or not, and then discusses a specific place concerning the consideration of the word (اعتبار اللفظ). The author explains that if a dialectician uses a term not commonly known for a meaning, or uses an equivocal term with multiple meanings, the obligation is to follow common usage (الجمهور) in naming. Naming is of two types: direct (بغير واسطة) and mediated (بواسطة). The dialectician must be cautious with equivocal terms: the refuter (مبطل) only needs to find one meaning where the judgment fails to refute a universal claim, while the prover (مثبت) must establish the judgment for all meanings. An example is given: 'Every human soul does not die' vs 'Some human soul does not die'—the dispute is the same because the particular is in wording not meaning. Then the author discusses the predicate (محمول) being equivocal (اشتراك), univocal (تواطؤ), or analogical (تشكيك). Examples of analogical terms include 'existent' applied to different things, and 'knowledge' applied to different relations. Finally, the author classifies places into three types: from the subjects of the subject, from what is said of the predicate, and from what the predicate is said of. Some places are shared between dialectic and demonstration, others not. The author concludes that the previous discussion suffices for proof and refutation.

Key Terms
موضع (mawḍiʿ): A dialectical or demonstrative place; a pattern of reasoning used in argumentation to prove or refute a claim. [Note: In earlier logic, mawḍiʿ often refers to the subject of a proposition; here it is used as a technical term for argumentative loci.]
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Equivocation: a single word having multiple meanings, used in dialectical arguments. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
جدلي (jadaliyy): Dialectical: pertaining to dialectic (jadal), which uses generally accepted premises rather than certain ones. [Note: In the logical tradition, jadal is one of the five arts; its meaning is consistent.]
تواطؤ (tawāṭuʾ): Univocity: a term applied to multiple things in exactly the same sense. [Note: Stable.]
تشكيك (tashkīk): Analogy: a term applied to multiple things in different ways, such as priority and posteriority (e.g., existence applied to substance and accident). [Note: In some later texts, tashkīk is used more specifically for graded analogy; here it is broader.]
مغالطي (mughāliṭiyy): Sophistical: pertaining to sophistry, where fallacious arguments are used deliberately. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the obligation of a dialectician when naming a meaning?
A) He must invent a new name to avoid confusion.
B) He must follow the common people in naming meanings.
C) He can use any name as long as it is clear to himself.
D) He must use the name that is most philosophical.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical argument, the burden is on the speaker to use terms in their commonly understood sense, not to invent new terminology without justification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In a dialectical debate about the validity of a contract, one party calls it 'bay' (sale) but uses an unusual definition. Explain why the dialectician must follow the common understanding of 'bay' and how this affects the argument.
?
Task: Given the statement 'This is a horse' where 'horse' is the well-known name for the animal, identify the type of naming (direct or mediated) according to the passage.

The Refuter and Prover with Equivocal Terms
Dialectical/sophistical place with equivocal terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the burden of proof for a prover (مثبت) versus a refuter (مبطل) when an equivocal term is used in a universal claim.

Core Idea
To refute a universal claim with an equivocal term, one counterexample suffices; to prove it, all meanings must be established.

The author explains a dialectical place that can also be used sophistically: when a dialectician uses an equivocal term and makes a universal judgment. The refuter only needs to find one meaning where the judgment fails, while the prover must show it holds for all meanings. This asymmetry is crucial in dialectical strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal claim using an equivocal term
Find the statement that is being argued for or against, where the key term has multiple meanings.
Example: 'Every human soul does not die' – 'soul' might be equivocal.
2
Determine your role: prover or refuter
If you are the prover (مثبت), you need to establish the judgment for all meanings of the equivocal term. If you are the refuter (مبطل), you only need to find one meaning where the judgment fails.
The passage says: 'As for the refuter, it suffices for him to show in some of those meanings that the judgment does not exist.'
3
If refuting, find a counterexample in one meaning
Select one meaning of the equivocal term and show that the universal claim does not hold for that meaning.
The passage adds that this works if the interlocutor is unaware of the equivocation (sophistical use).
4
If proving, establish the judgment for all meanings
You must check every distinct meaning of the term and demonstrate that the claim is true in each case.
The author gives an example: 'every human soul does not die' vs 'some human soul does not die' – the dispute is the same because the particularity is in wording, not meaning. The prover must prove it for all souls.

Source Text

According to the passage, what must a refuter (مبطل) do to invalidate a universal claim that uses an equivocal term?
A) Show that the judgment fails for all meanings of the term.
B) Show that the judgment fails for at least one meaning of the term.
C) Show that the term has no meaning at all.
D) Show that the prover is lying.

Types of Predicates: Univocity, Equivocation, and Analogy
Classification of predicates by semantic type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a predicate as univocal, equivocal, or analogical using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Knowing whether a term is univocal, equivocal, or analogical determines the validity of inferences involving that term.

The author concludes the passage by classifying predicates into three types: univocal (تواطؤ), equivocal (اشتراك), and analogical (تشكيك). These distinctions are essential for sound reasoning, as they affect how a predicate relates to its subject. This lesson explores each type with Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate in a statement
Find the term that is being predicated of the subject. For example, 'existent' in 'Allah is existent' and 'the world is existent'.
2
Check if the predicate applies in exactly the same sense (univocal)
If the predicate means exactly the same in all its applications, it is univocal (تواطؤ). Example: 'wajib' in 'prayer is wajib' and 'zakat is wajib'.
The passage says: 'قد لا يكون بل يكون بالتواطؤ' – or it may be by univocity.
3
Check if the predicate applies in completely different senses (equivocal)
If the predicate has distinct, unrelated meanings in different contexts, it is equivocal (اشتراك). Example: 'ayn' meaning eye, spring, or essence.
The passage discusses equivocation earlier.
4
Check if the predicate applies in related but different ways (analogical)
If the predicate applies to things according to priority and posteriority, or with a relation, it is analogical (تشكيك). Example: 'existent' applied to Allah and creation, 'knowledge' applied to knowledge of health and knowledge of opposites.
The passage gives examples: 'الموجود على الأشياء المختلفة في الحقيقة' – 'existent' applied to things different in reality, and 'العلم بالشيء' – knowledge of a thing as a purpose or principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بعد نحو اعتبار اللفظ ونافع في الإثبات والإبطال وهو أنه إن كان لم يأتِ للمعنى بالاسم المشهور له بل اخترع من نفسه اسما. أما على سبيل اشتراك فيه إذا كان لذلك الاسم عند الجمهور معنى آخر ؛ والواجب علينا اتباع الجمهور في التسمية للمعاني والتسمية على وجهين تسمية بغير واسطة كتسمية معنى الحيوان حيوانًا، وتسمية بواسطة كتسمية الجسم الفلاني حيوانا؛ وذلك بأن يجعل الشيء داخلا تحت المعنى الذي له الاسم أولا، وليس هذا أي الجمهور بل الجمهور لم يلتفتوا إلى ما لا يكون دالا على المعنى في المتعارف. فإذا ثبت أن هذا اللفظ صالح المتعارف لهذا المعنى فحينئذ يجب أن يُلتفت إليه ؛ وهذا الموضع جدلي أيضًا. وموضع آخر يمكن أن يكون جدليا، ويمكن أن يكون مغالطيّا. وذلك لأن الجدلي إذا استعمل لفظاً مشتركا ثم حكم عليه كلي لم يتبين استمراره في جميع تلك المعاني. وإنما يمكنه ذلك إذا كان المخاطب لا يشعر باشتراك الاسم؛ وهذا الموضع صالح للإثبات والإبطال. وأما المبطل فيكفيه أن يتبين في بعض تلك المعاني أن الحكم غير موجود إذا كان يبطل كليا. وأما المثبت فيحتاج إلى أن يثبت في الكل ثمَّ من الجزئي ما يكون في حكم الكلي حتى يثبت الكلي بإثبات الحكم في الجزئي كما ينتفي بنفي الحكم عن الجزئي؛ وذلك فيما يكون الخلاف في الكل والبعض واحدًا مثلا إذا قيل كل نفس إنسانية لا تموت فإنه في الخلاف مع قولنا بعض النفس الإنسانية لا تموت واحدا، فلذلك حكم ما يكون جزئياً ليس جزئياً في المعنى بل في اللفظ. وبالجملة فالمحمول قد يكون بالاشتراك، وقد لا يكون بل يكون بالتواطؤ كما مر ، أو بالتشكيك كالموجود على الأشياء المختلفة في الحقيقة؛ وحينئذ يمكن أن يقع على البعض منها أولا وعلى البعض ثانيا، وقد يكون يوقع من التشكيك مختلطا كما أن اللفظ يدل على النسبة؛ والنسبة ليست كلها إلى غاية أو إلى مبدأ بل مثل العلم بالشيء غاية تعم ما يكون علما بما هو له كالمبدأ نحو العلم بالصحة وبما له الغاية / نحو العلم بالمتضادات. فيكون العلم [٣٢٩ ] بالشيء يعم كل واحد منهما عموم التشكيك ، وقد يكون بالذات وبالعرض أيضًا كالشهوة للشيء، فإن لفظة تكون على أنها غاية كالصحة، وعلى أنها مبدأ كالمداواة، وعلى ما بالذات كمن يشتهي الحلو لأنه حلو، وعلى ما بعرض كمن يشتهي الشراب لا لأنه شراب بل لأنه حلو؛ فيقال لجميع هذا مشتهى في التشكيك. ثم من المواضع ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدلي والبرهان، ومنها ما لا يكون مشتركا كما مر ، والموضع إما موضع من موضوعات الموضوع، أو موضع مما يقال على المحمول قولا، أو موضع مما يقال عليه المحمول قولا. وإن اتفق أن يتشارك بعض هذه المواضع في الموضوع فالاعتبار مختلف؛ وقد علمت أنه كيف يصلح ما سلف للإثبات والإبطال فلا حاجة إلى البيان.
It can be fixed, and it can not be. And if the questioner presents a syllogism in response to what the answerer posits as a syllogism, that is not an invalidation of the thing in itself, but rather a proof that entails invalidation. And know that of this kind also is a place concerning consideration of the word, useful in proof and refutation: that if he did not bring for the meaning its well-known name but invented a name for himself, either on the basis of equivocation if that name has another meaning among the common people; and it is obligatory upon us to follow the common people in naming meanings. And naming is of two types: naming without mediation, like naming the meaning of 'animal' as animal, and naming with mediation, like naming a particular body as animal, by making the thing fall under the meaning that has the name first. And this is not the common people, rather the common people do not pay attention to what does not indicate the meaning in conventional usage. So if it is established that this word is conventionally suitable for this meaning, then it must be attended to. And this place is also dialectical. Another place can be dialectical and can be sophistical. That is because the dialectician, if he uses an equivocal word and then makes a universal judgment, it does not become clear that it applies continuously to all those meanings. He can only do that if the interlocutor is unaware of the equivocation of the name; this place is suitable for proof and refutation. As for the refuter, it suffices for him to show in some of those meanings that the judgment does not exist if he is refuting a universal. As for the prover, he needs to establish in all, then from the particular what is in the status of the universal, so that the universal is established by establishing the judgment in the particular just as it is negated by negating the judgment from the particular. That is in cases where the dispute concerning the universal and the particular is one, for example if it is said 'Every human soul does not die', then in dispute with our saying 'Some human souls do not die' it is one; therefore the judgment of what is particular is not particular in meaning but in wording. In summary, the predicate may be by equivocation, or may not be but by univocity as previously, or by analogy like 'existent' applied to things different in reality; and then it can be applied to some of them first and to others second, and it may be a mixed analogy just as the word indicates a relation; and the relation is not all to a purpose or to a principle, but like knowledge of a thing as a purpose that includes what is knowledge of what is for it as a principle, like knowledge of health and knowledge of opposites. So knowledge of a thing includes each of them by analogical generality, and it may be essential and accidental as well, like desire for a thing: the word can be as a purpose like health, as a principle like medication, as essential like one who desires sweetness because it is sweet, and as accidental like one who desires drink not because it is drink but because it is sweet; so all of this is called 'desired' by analogy. Then among the places are those shared between the dialectical and the demonstrative, and among them are those not shared as previously. The place is either a place from the subjects of the subject, or a place from what is said of the predicate, or a place from what the predicate is said of. And if it happens that some of these places share in the subject, the consideration is different; and you have known how what preceded is suitable for proof and refutation, so there is no need for clarification.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on dialectical places (مواضع جدلية) focused on the role of word usage in proof and refutation. It begins by noting that a place can be either fixed or not, and then discusses a specific place concerning the consideration of the word (اعتبار اللفظ). The author explains that if a dialectician uses a term not commonly known for a meaning, or uses an equivocal term with multiple meanings, the obligation is to follow common usage (الجمهور) in naming. Naming is of two types: direct (بغير واسطة) and mediated (بواسطة). The dialectician must be cautious with equivocal terms: the refuter (مبطل) only needs to find one meaning where the judgment fails to refute a universal claim, while the prover (مثبت) must establish the judgment for all meanings. An example is given: 'Every human soul does not die' vs 'Some human soul does not die'—the dispute is the same because the particular is in wording not meaning. Then the author discusses the predicate (محمول) being equivocal (اشتراك), univocal (تواطؤ), or analogical (تشكيك). Examples of analogical terms include 'existent' applied to different things, and 'knowledge' applied to different relations. Finally, the author classifies places into three types: from the subjects of the subject, from what is said of the predicate, and from what the predicate is said of. Some places are shared between dialectic and demonstration, others not. The author concludes that the previous discussion suffices for proof and refutation.

Key Terms
تواطؤ (tawāṭuʾ): Univocity: a term applied to multiple things in exactly the same sense. [Note: Stable.]
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Equivocation: a single word having multiple meanings, used in dialectical arguments. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
تشكيك (tashkīk): Analogy: a term applied to multiple things in different ways, such as priority and posteriority (e.g., existence applied to substance and accident). [Note: In some later texts, tashkīk is used more specifically for graded analogy; here it is broader.]
محمول (maḥmūl): Predicate: that which is affirmed or denied of a subject in a proposition. [Note: Standard logical term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of an analogical predicate (تشكيك)?
A) 'Wajib' applied to prayer and zakat (univocal).
B) 'Existent' applied to God and creation.
C) 'Ayn' meaning eye and spring (equivocal).
D) 'Human' applied to Zaid and Amr (univocal).

Key Takeaway
Distinguishing univocal, equivocal, and analogical predicates is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences, as it determines how arguments involving those terms are evaluated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Consider the term 'knowledge' (علم) in the following statements: 'knowledge of health' and 'knowledge of opposites'. According to the passage, is this univocal, equivocal, or analogical? Explain using the author's examples.
?

External Matters: Shared Topics in Dialectic and Demonstration
Explains external matters shared between jadal and burhan with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify external matters (al-umūr al-khārija) in a given argument and classify them by their use in transition (intiqāl) for affirmation or refutation.

Core Idea
External matters link a claim to its implications, enabling dialectical and demonstrative reasoning.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of external matters—topics that lie outside the immediate subject but are used to support reasoning. These are shared between dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhān). Understanding external matters is crucial for recognizing how arguments often move from a claim to its necessary conditions (malzūmāt) or implications (lawāzim), a technique common in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the statement under discussion—the 'required' (maṭlūb) that the argument centers on.
In the passage, the examples are about the animal that feeds but does not grow, and the disjunction of sun and night.
2
Detect the transition
Look for a shift from the claim to an external matter—such as time, implication, or opposition. The author calls this intiqāl.
The external matter may be a necessary condition (malzūm) or an implication (lāzim) of the claim.
3
Classify the external matter
Determine whether it is based on necessity (luzūm) or opposition ('anād). The passage gives examples of both.
The time example (animal growth) is based on necessity; the disjunctive example (sun or night) is based on opposition.
4
Determine the direction
Is the external matter used for affirmation (ithbāt) or refutation (ibṭāl)? The text says external matters are often used in refutation.
For example, if a claim's necessary condition is false, the claim is refuted.
5
Check the relationship
Ensure the external matter has a real, well-known connection to the claim; otherwise it becomes a fallacy.
The author warns that without a genuine relationship, the transition is among the 'mughālaṭāt' (fallacies).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنهما من المواضع من جملة ما يكون مشتركا بين الجدل والبرهان. وقد ينتفع من الأمور الخارجة باعتبار الزمان كما يقال مثلا كل مغتذ تام ثم نجد الحيوان الواقف في السِّنِّ والنحط يغتذي في ذلك الزمان ولا ينمو ; وهذا أيضًا يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان. وينتفع في الإبطال دون الإثبات أو كل ما اختلف زمانه فهو مختلف، وليس كل ما اتفق زمانه فهو متفق; فهذا الموضوع من جملة المواضع الخارجة عن حدي المطلوب. وكثيرا ما يقع الانتقال من الكلام في الشيء إلى الكلام في أمور خارجة في ملزوماته أو لوازم تكون إذا صحت أو بطلت انتقل منها إلى الحكم في الشيء. فربَّما كان ذلك الانتقال ضروريًا في الحقيقة حيث يكون القياس المنتقل إليه أو الاستقراء المنتقل إليه ليصحح حال لازم أو ملزوم، أو إبانة صدق الاستثناء فيه ضروريًا في الحقيقة. وربما كانا ضروريتين في ظاهر الأمر وفي المشهور. وكلاهما مقبول في الجدل; ويجب أن يكون الانتقال إلى اللوازم والملزومات التي بينها وبين المطلوب علاقة حقيقية مشهورة مقبولة في الظاهر وإلا لكان من المغالطات. ثم كل شيء يقال فله لوازم كثيرة غاية الكثرة; وملزومات كذلك. فكل من قال شيئًا بالفعل فنفسه أن يكون قال أشياء بالقوة. وأنت تعلم كيف ينفع اعتبار اللوازم والملزومات في الإثبات والإبطال، ويكون من الأمور الخارجة النافعة ما ليس على سبيل اللزوم بل على سبيل العناد كما في قولهم إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودًا، فإنه مما ينتفع به بطريق الاستثناء في الإثبات / والإبطال; وإنه مما يشترك فيه الجدل والبرهان أيضًا.
And both of them are among the topics that are shared between dialectic and demonstration. And one may benefit from external matters by consideration of time, as for example it is said: every complete feeder, then we find the animal that has stopped in age and decline feeds at that time but does not grow; and this is also shared between dialectic and demonstration. And it is used in refutation rather than affirmation: or everything whose time differs is different, but not everything whose time agrees is agreed; so this subject is among the external topics outside the two limits of the required. And often the transition occurs from speech about a thing to speech about external matters in its necessary conditions or implications that, if they are true or false, one moves from them to judgment about the thing. So perhaps that transition is necessary in reality where the syllogism transferred to or the induction transferred to, in order to correct the state of an implication or a necessary condition, or to show the truth of the exception therein, is necessary in reality. And perhaps both are necessary in appearance and in common opinion. And both are acceptable in dialectic; and it is necessary that the transition be to implications and necessary conditions between which and the required there is a real relationship well-known and acceptable in appearance, otherwise it would be among the fallacies. Then everything that is said has many implications, extremely many; and necessary conditions likewise. So everyone who says something actually, by his very self he has said things potentially. And you know how the consideration of implications and necessary conditions benefits in affirmation and refutation. And among the beneficial external matters is what is not by way of necessity but by way of opposition, as in their saying: either the sun is rising or the night exists; for it is benefited by way of exception in affirmation and refutation; and it is also shared between dialectic and demonstration.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the concept of 'external matters' (al-umūr al-khārija) that are common to both dialectical (jadal) and demonstrative (burhān) reasoning. The author begins by stating that these topics are shared between the two arts. He provides an example concerning time: a fully grown animal that has ceased growing in old age still feeds but does not grow—this temporal aspect is an external matter used in argument. He then notes that such external matters are used more often in refutation (ibṭāl) than in affirmation (ithbāt). The principle is that if times differ, the thing differs, but sameness of time does not guarantee sameness. Next, he discusses the transition (intiqāl) from speaking about a thing to speaking about external matters that are its necessary conditions (malzūmāt) or implications (lawāzim). Such transitions can be necessary in reality or merely in appearance/ common opinion, but both are acceptable in dialectic. He warns that the relationship must be real and well-known, otherwise it becomes fallacious. Every statement has many implications and necessary conditions; one who says something actually also says many things potentially. Finally, he introduces a special type of external matter that is not based on necessity (luzūm) but on opposition ('anād), exemplified by the disjunction 'either the sun is rising or the night exists'. This is used in conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī) and is also shared between dialectic and demonstration.

Key Terms
الأمور الخارجة
اللوازم
الملزومات
الاستثناء

Key Takeaway
External matters are topics outside the immediate subject that, through a genuine relationship of implication or opposition, allow valid transitions in both dialectical and demonstrative reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Art of Name Transfer in Dialectical Clarification
Explains name transfer as a dialectical tool for clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify attribute-based names that carry additional connotations, and determine when transferring the name is necessary to avoid confusion.

Core Idea
Names based on attributes often smuggle hidden extras—transfer them to isolate the core meaning.

The transfer and replacement of names (naql al-ism wa tabdīluh) is a key dialectical technique for clarification, especially when names derive from attributes rather than essences. This lesson will help you recognize when a name implies more than its apparent definition, and how to handle that extra connotation in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify attribute-based names
Look for names that describe a quality (e.g., 'the brave') rather than an essence (e.g., 'human'). These often carry hidden assumptions.
The passage contrasts attribute-based (bi-ḥasab al-ṣifa) with essence-based (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt).
2
Extract the core meaning
Strip away the name and consider the meaning in itself. Ask: what is the essential property? For 'the brave', it is 'good-souled' (jayyid al-nafs).
This isolation reveals whether extra connotations are present.
3
Check for added connotation
Compare the name with its core meaning. Does the name suggest something extra? The passage notes that 'shujā‘' implies more than just 'jayyid al-nafs'.
The extra connotation may affect the judgment.
4
Decide if the name matters
Determine whether using the original name or its transferred version changes the argument. If the extra connotation is relevant, the name must be carefully defined.
This step repels confusion (talbīs) and speeds discovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة اعتبار الأمور الخارجة نقل الاسم وتبديله فربَّما يقع في البيان وخصوصًا إذا كان الاسم بحسب الصفة لا بحسب الذات; فيجب أن نأخذ المعنى ونلحظه في ذاته ونقتصر الاسم عليه. فإن تعدل دلالة الاسم إلى ما هو مباين للمعنى بوجه فإنه أنقص منه، أو أزيد عليه، أو هو شرط دل عليه وعرف; وهذا كما يؤخذ الشجاع وجيد النفس على أن المفهوم منهما واحد، وعلى أن جيد النفس هو قول تحت اسم الشجاع، وعلى أن الكلام فيهما واحد ثمَّ يتأدى به الاسمان في النظر إلى الاشتغال بأحدهما من حيث له زيادة اعتبار بأن استعمال أحدهما يكفي فيما نحن فيه أو لا يكفي; فإن لفظ الشجاع يوهم شيئًا زائدا على أنه جيد النفس فقط. وفي تلك الزيادة يعتبر في الحكم; وهذا الموضع نافع في دفع التلبيس وسرعة الكشف. وموضع آخر ليس يعتبر فيه الوجود; وذلك لأن الشيء كونه موجودًا للموضوع غير كونه له دائمًا أو أكثريا أو أقليا، وغير أنه لكله أو لبعضه، وغير كونه له بالنسبة إلى كذا أو ليس إذا سلم وجود فقد سلم من كل وجه فيجب أن نراعي في ذلك أن لا نأخذ أحد الحكمين مكان الآخر، وأن لا نمنع بمطلق غير مبين بل نطالب القابل المجمل بالبيان ومما يشبه هذا الموضع قول من قال إن من اللذات ما هو فرح، ومنه ما هو سرور، ومنه ما هو خذل; وهذه كلها ألفاظ مترادفة لا بد من الاستكشاف.
And among the consideration of external matters is the transfer and replacement of the name; it may occur in clarification, especially when the name is according to attribute, not according to essence. So it is necessary that we take the meaning and consider it in itself and restrict the name to it. For if the denotation of the name is altered to what is divergent from the meaning in some respect, then it is either less than it, or more than it, or it is a condition indicated and known. And this is like when one takes 'the brave' and 'the good-souled' as that the understood meaning of them is one, and that 'good-souled' is an expression under the name 'brave', and that the speech about them is one; then the two names lead in consideration to occupying oneself with one of them insofar as it has an additional consideration, whether using one of them suffices in what we are in or not. For the word 'the brave' suggests something extra beyond it being merely 'good-souled'. And in that extra, it is considered in the judgment; and this position is beneficial in repelling confusion and rapidity of discovery. And another position in which existence is not considered; and that is because the thing's being existent for the subject is different from its being for it always or mostly or rarely, and different from its being for all of it or part of it, and different from its being for it in relation to such-and-such or not. If existence is conceded, then it is conceded from every aspect; so we must take care in that not to take one of the two judgments in place of another, and not to prevent with an absolute unspecified, but rather we demand the one who accepts the general to clarify. And similar to this position is the saying of someone who said: among pleasures are joy, delight, and glee; all these are synonymous expressions—discovery is necessary.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage discusses another type of external matter: the transfer and replacement of names (naql al-ism wa tabdīluh). The author explains that this technique is particularly useful in clarification (bayān) when the name is based on an attribute (ṣifa) rather than on essence (dhāt). In such cases, we must isolate the meaning and consider it in itself, restricting the name to that meaning. If the name is altered to something divergent (mubāyin) from the meaning, it may be deficient, excessive, or conditional. The author illustrates with the example of 'the brave' (shujā‘) and 'the good-souled' (jayyid al-nafs). While they are often taken as synonymous, shujā‘ carries an additional connotation beyond merely being good-souled. This extra connotation affects the judgment. Recognizing this helps in avoiding confusion (talbīs) and achieving rapid discovery (sur‘at al-kashf). Next, the author introduces another position where existence (wujūd) is not considered as a factor. Here, the thing's existence for the subject is distinguished from its permanence, frequency, or rarity, and from its being universal or partial. If existence is conceded, it is conceded from every aspect, so one must not confuse judgments or reject without specification. Finally, the author gives an example of synonymous expressions like 'pleasure' (ladhah), 'joy' (farah), 'delight' (surūr), and 'glee' (khadhal), stating that these require careful distinction.

Key Terms
نقل الاسم
الشجاع
جيد النفس

C) They are synonymous but shujā‘ suggests an additional quality.
D) Jayyid al-nafs is a genus of shujā‘.

Key Takeaway
Name transfer reveals hidden connotations in attribute-based terms, a crucial step for precise argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the usuli example of 'qat‘' (decisive evidence) and 'zann' (probable evidence). Identify the attribute-based core meaning of each and check if the name implies any extra connotation beyond the degree of certainty. Then decide whether using the name alone suffices in a legal argument.

Existence vs. Modality: Avoiding Judgment Confusion
Explains the distinction between existence and other modalities to prevent judgment confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the existence of a property and its frequency, duration, or scope, and demand precise clarification when required.

Core Idea
Existence alone concedes the whole; do not confuse with how often or how much.

The passage shifts to another external matter: when existence is not a factor, we must distinguish it from permanence, frequency, universality, or relativity. This lesson trains you to separate sheer existence from other modalities and to call for clear specification in debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the existence claim
Recognize when an argument asserts that something exists for a subject (e.g., 'Zayd has knowledge'). The passage says existence is conceded from every aspect if it is simply conceded.
Existence here means the attribute is present, not its degree.
2
Distinguish existence from modality
Note that existence is different from 'always', 'mostly', 'rarely', 'for all', 'for some', or 'in relation to something else'. The passage lists these distinctions explicitly.
Do not treat a modal claim as equivalent to a simple existence claim.
3
Avoid taking one judgment for another
If someone concedes existence, do not assume they also concede frequency or universality. The passage warns: 'فيجب أن نراعي في ذلك أن لا نأخذ أحد الحكمين مكان الآخر' (we must take care not to take one judgment in place of another).
This is a common dialectical mistake.
4
Demand clarification when needed
When an opponent uses absolute unspecified terms, demand precise clarification. The passage says: 'نطالب القابل المجمل بالبيان' (we demand that the one who accepts the general to clarify).
This prevents ambiguity and forces specificity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جملة اعتبار الأمور الخارجة نقل الاسم وتبديله فربَّما يقع في البيان وخصوصًا إذا كان الاسم بحسب الصفة لا بحسب الذات; فيجب أن نأخذ المعنى ونلحظه في ذاته ونقتصر الاسم عليه. فإن تعدل دلالة الاسم إلى ما هو مباين للمعنى بوجه فإنه أنقص منه، أو أزيد عليه، أو هو شرط دل عليه وعرف; وهذا كما يؤخذ الشجاع وجيد النفس على أن المفهوم منهما واحد، وعلى أن جيد النفس هو قول تحت اسم الشجاع، وعلى أن الكلام فيهما واحد ثمَّ يتأدى به الاسمان في النظر إلى الاشتغال بأحدهما من حيث له زيادة اعتبار بأن استعمال أحدهما يكفي فيما نحن فيه أو لا يكفي; فإن لفظ الشجاع يوهم شيئًا زائدا على أنه جيد النفس فقط. وفي تلك الزيادة يعتبر في الحكم; وهذا الموضع نافع في دفع التلبيس وسرعة الكشف. وموضع آخر ليس يعتبر فيه الوجود; وذلك لأن الشيء كونه موجودًا للموضوع غير كونه له دائمًا أو أكثريا أو أقليا، وغير أنه لكله أو لبعضه، وغير كونه له بالنسبة إلى كذا أو ليس إذا سلم وجود فقد سلم من كل وجه فيجب أن نراعي في ذلك أن لا نأخذ أحد الحكمين مكان الآخر، وأن لا نمنع بمطلق غير مبين بل نطالب القابل المجمل بالبيان ومما يشبه هذا الموضع قول من قال إن من اللذات ما هو فرح، ومنه ما هو سرور، ومنه ما هو خذل; وهذه كلها ألفاظ مترادفة لا بد من الاستكشاف.
And among the consideration of external matters is the transfer and replacement of the name; it may occur in clarification, especially when the name is according to attribute, not according to essence. So it is necessary that we take the meaning and consider it in itself and restrict the name to it. For if the denotation of the name is altered to what is divergent from the meaning in some respect, then it is either less than it, or more than it, or it is a condition indicated and known. And this is like when one takes 'the brave' and 'the good-souled' as that the understood meaning of them is one, and that 'good-souled' is an expression under the name 'brave', and that the speech about them is one; then the two names lead in consideration to occupying oneself with one of them insofar as it has an additional consideration, whether using one of them suffices in what we are in or not. For the word 'the brave' suggests something extra beyond it being merely 'good-souled'. And in that extra, it is considered in the judgment; and this position is beneficial in repelling confusion and rapidity of discovery. And another position in which existence is not considered; and that is because the thing's being existent for the subject is different from its being for it always or mostly or rarely, and different from its being for all of it or part of it, and different from its being for it in relation to such-and-such or not. If existence is conceded, then it is conceded from every aspect; so we must take care in that not to take one of the two judgments in place of another, and not to prevent with an absolute unspecified, but rather we demand the one who accepts the general to clarify. And similar to this position is the saying of someone who said: among pleasures are joy, delight, and glee; all these are synonymous expressions—discovery is necessary.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage discusses another type of external matter: the transfer and replacement of names (naql al-ism wa tabdīluh). The author explains that this technique is particularly useful in clarification (bayān) when the name is based on an attribute (ṣifa) rather than on essence (dhāt). In such cases, we must isolate the meaning and consider it in itself, restricting the name to that meaning. If the name is altered to something divergent (mubāyin) from the meaning, it may be deficient, excessive, or conditional. The author illustrates with the example of 'the brave' (shujā‘) and 'the good-souled' (jayyid al-nafs). While they are often taken as synonymous, shujā‘ carries an additional connotation beyond merely being good-souled. This extra connotation affects the judgment. Recognizing this helps in avoiding confusion (talbīs) and achieving rapid discovery (sur‘at al-kashf). Next, the author introduces another position where existence (wujūd) is not considered as a factor. Here, the thing's existence for the subject is distinguished from its permanence, frequency, or rarity, and from its being universal or partial. If existence is conceded, it is conceded from every aspect, so one must not confuse judgments or reject without specification. Finally, the author gives an example of synonymous expressions like 'pleasure' (ladhah), 'joy' (farah), 'delight' (surūr), and 'glee' (khadhal), stating that these require careful distinction.

الوجود
دائمًا
أكثريا
أقليا

Key Takeaway
Never treat a concession of existence as a concession of any modal claim; demand clear specification.

Dialectical Conditionals from Mutual Opposites
Introduces dialectical topics from mutual opposites for conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and evaluate a dialectical conditional syllogism derived from mutual opposites.

Core Idea
Dialectical conditionals from contraries are plausible but never necessary.

In Islamic logic, dialectical syllogisms rely on commonly accepted premises rather than certainties. This passage introduces a technique: using pairs of opposites to build conditional arguments. Understanding the limits of such arguments—they are not necessary—prevents you from mistaking dialectical plausibility for logical certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs of opposites
In the example, the author uses friends/enemies and kindness/harm. Find two contrasting pairs in the passage. These are the contraries (ضدان) from which the conditional is built.
The pairs can be any set of opposites, e.g., good/bad, just/unjust.
2
Construct the conditional statement
Form a conditional: 'If [property 1] applies to [first member of pair 1], then [property 2] applies to [second member of pair 2].' The author gives: 'If kindness to friends is beautiful, then harm to enemies is beautiful.'
The property (beautiful/ugly) is taken from the same domain.
3
Test the necessity of the conditional
The author states such conditionals are not necessary (لا تكون واجبة). Try to conceive a counterexample. Could kindness to friends be beautiful while harm to enemies is not? That shows lack of logical necessity.
The reasoning depends on the opposites being contraries, not contradictories.
4
Recognise the dialectical limitation
The passage warns: just because a predicate holds for every member of one pair does not mean its opposite holds for the opposite member. This is a common fallacy in dialectical reasoning.
These are 'topics' (مواضع جدلية), not proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا مواضع خارجة مأخوذة من المتقابلات ومن أحوال الابتداء والانتهاء ومن الاشتقاقات وغير ذلك. وكأنها تنفع بحسب القياسات الاستثنائية المتّصلة: أما ما يكون من المتقابلات الضدية فطريقه أن يؤخذ متقابلان من الأضداد؛ و متقابلان منها كذلك مؤلّف على أنحاء التي نذكرها، ونجعل منها مقدم وتال فليكن مقابل هو مقابل الأصدقاء والأعداء، والآخر مقابل الإساءة والإحسان فيقال إن كان الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء جميلاً فالإساءة إلى الأعداء جميلًا، أو يقال إن كانت الإساءة إلى الأصدقاء قبيحًا فالإحسان إلى الأعداء جميلا. وعلى هذا طردًا وعكسا؛ فهذه كلها لا تكون واجبة، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء موجودًا لكل الضد أن يكون ضده موجودًا للضد؛ ومثل هذه المواضع من المواضع الجدلية لا غير.
And here are external topics taken from opposites and from states of beginning and ending, from derivations, and other things. They are beneficial according to connected conditional syllogisms. As for what is from mutual opposites (i.e., contraries), its method is that two opposites from contraries are taken, and two opposites from them are composed in the ways we will mention, and we make from them an antecedent and a consequent. Let one opposite be the opposite of friends and enemies, and the other opposite the opposite of kindness and harm. So it is said: 'If being kind to friends is beautiful, then being harmful to enemies is beautiful.' Or it is said: 'If being harmful to friends is ugly, then being kind to enemies is beautiful.' And this in direct and reverse order. All these are not necessary, nor does it follow from something's being a property of every one of two opposites that its opposite is a property of the opposite. Such topics are only dialectical topics.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage introduces a set of 'external topics' derived from various relations, including mutual opposites (contraries), and explains how they are used in connected conditional syllogisms (القياسات الاستثنائية المتّصلة). The author gives an example: take two pairs of opposites—friends/enemies and kindness/harm. From these, one can form conditionals like 'If kindness to friends is beautiful, then harm to enemies is beautiful.' However, the author emphasizes that such conditionals are not necessary; they are merely dialectical (جدلية). The passage makes a crucial logical point: just because something holds for one of a pair of opposites, it does not necessarily hold for the opposite of the other. This is an expansion probe that explains a dialectical technique, not a demonstration of scientific proof.

Key Terms
المتقابلات الضدية
قياسات استثنائية متّصلة
مواضع جدلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Dialectical conditionals from mutual opposites are persuasive but lack logical necessity – you cannot infer the opposite of the consequent from the antecedent by mere contrariety.

Opposites as a Dialectical Locus
Explains opposites as a dialectical locus and the condition of being well-known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a premise about opposites can be used in dialectical reasoning based on its being well-known (mashhur).

Core Idea
In dialectic, truth is secondary; well-known acceptance is decisive.

This lesson explores how opposites function as a dialectical topic. The passage explains that judgments about opposites are not self-evident because there is debate—for instance, whether one thing can have multiple opposites. In dialectical reasoning, the criterion is not absolute truth but whether the claim is well-known (mashhur) among the disputants. Understanding this distinction helps you separate dialectical arguments from demonstrative proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical topic
The passage introduces 'opposites' (al-aḍdād) as one of the dialectical loci (mawāḍiʿ jadaliyya). Recognise that such topics are used only in dialectic, not in demonstration.
Dialectical loci are starting points for dialectical arguments.
2
Recognise the underlying uncertainty
The text says 'al-aḥkām fī al-aḍdād ghayru bayyin' – judgments concerning opposites are not clear. This is because there is a genuine controversy: can a single thing have multiple opposites? (e.g., can 'black' have opposites like 'white' and 'red'?)
This uncertainty is what makes the topic suitable for dialectic, where certainty is not required.
3
Apply the well-known criterion
In dialectic, what is considered is what is well-known (mashhūr). The passage states: 'yuʿtabaru fī al-jadal mā yakūnu mashhūran.' So even if the logical truth is unclear, the premise is acceptable if it is widely accepted.
Well-known premises are the building blocks of dialectical arguments.
4
Contrast with demonstration
Demonstrative proof requires certain, self-evident premises. Dialectic only requires generally accepted opinions. This distinction prevents you from demanding demonstrative certainty in dialectical contexts.
Knowing the type of discourse prevents category errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من المواضع الجدلية لا غير. والأحكام في الأضداد غير بين؛ إذ فيه من الكلام أن للشيء الواحد هل يمكن أن تكون له أضداد كثيرة أم لا؛ فيعتبر في الجدل ما يكون مشهورًا بأنه كيف هو.
From the dialectical topics only. And judgments concerning opposites are not clear; for in it there is discussion whether a single thing can have multiple opposites or not; so in dialectic, what is considered is what is well-known as to how it is.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage identifies a specific dialectical topic: opposites (الأضداد). The author notes that rulings regarding opposites are not evident (غير بين) because there is debate whether one thing can have multiple opposites (e.g., can 'black' have multiple opposites like 'white' and 'red'?). In dialectical reasoning, what matters is not the absolute truth of the matter but whether the proposition is well-known (مشهور) among the disputants. The topic is used only as a dialectical locus, not for demonstration.

Key Terms
المواضع الجدلية
الأضداد
مشهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Opposites are a dialectical locus where the well-known opinion, not the logical truth, determines the premise's acceptability.

Dialectical Use of Contrapositive Conversion
Explains the use of contradictory pairs and the conversion of the contrapositive as a dialectical topic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a conditional proposition into its contrapositive and identify the standard of well-knownness in dialectical reasoning.

Core Idea
In dialectic, the contrapositive is valid if it is well-known, not necessarily demonstrative.

This passage explores the topic of contradiction in dialectic, focusing on the conversion of the contrapositive. It shows that a dialectical premise is acceptable if it is well-known (mashhur), even if not proven by demonstration. This is crucial for understanding how dialectical arguments differ from demonstrative ones in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional
Find the conditional statement in the passage. The author gives: 'If the pleasant is good' (إن كان اللذيذ حسنا). This is the antecedent, and the consequent is implicit: 'then what is not good is not pleasant'.
The conditionals are about the relationship between 'good' and 'pleasant'.
2
Form the contrapositive
The contrapositive of 'If P then Q' is 'If not Q then not P'. In the example, from 'If pleasant is good' we derive 'If not good then not pleasant'. Verify logical equivalence.
The second example is the converse of the contrapositive, which the author mentions may also be well-known.
3
Distinguish truth from well-knownness
The passage states that the contrapositive conversion is both true (حق) and well-known (مشهور). But the key is that in dialectic, the standard for acceptance is الشهرة (well-knownness), not absolute truth. A premise may be logically true but not well-known, or well-known but not demonstratively true.
The author explicitly says 'والمعتبر هو الشهرة' (what is considered is well-knownness).
4
Apply the dialectical standard
When using contradiction as a topic in debate, check whether the proposition (e.g., the contrapositive or its converse) is well-known among the interlocutors. If it is, it can be used even if it is not proven by demonstration.
This is why dialectical arguments are weaker than demonstrative ones but still persuasive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الغير من المتقابلات نحو التناقض فمن جملة ذلك ما هو حق ومشهور معا؛ وهو جعل التالي عكس نقيض المقدم، أو جعل نقيض التالي مقدما لنقيض المقدم فيقال مثلا إن كان اللذيذ حسنا فيما ليس بحسن ليس بلذيذ، أو كان ما ليس بحسن بلذيذ فكل لذيذ حسن. وأما عكس هذا فربما كان مشهورًا؛ والمعتبر هو الشهرة.
And as for the other [type] of correlatives like contradiction, among that is what is true and well-known together; that is, making the consequent the converse of the contradictory antecedent, or making the contradictory of the consequent an antecedent for the contradictory of the antecedent. So it is said, for example: 'If the pleasant is good, then what is not good is not pleasant' or 'If what is not good is pleasant, then every pleasant is good.' And as for the converse of this, it might be well-known; and what is considered is well-knownness.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage discusses the topic of contradiction (التناقض) within the class of correlatives (المتقابلات). The author points out that the conversion of the contrapositive (عكس نقيض المقدم) is both true (حق) and well-known (مشهور) in the dialectical sense. Two examples are given: (1) If the pleasant is good, then what is not good is not pleasant (the contrapositive: if not consequent then not antecedent). (2) If what is not good is pleasant, then every pleasant is good (this is the converse of the contrapositive, which is also valid). However, the author's emphasis is that in dialectic, the criterion is well-knownness (الشهرة), not absolute truth. So even if the converse of the contrapositive is not universally true in demonstration, it may still be used in dialectic if it is well-known.

Key Terms
التناقض
عكس نقيض المقدم
الشهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The premise must be demonstrated
B) The premise must be self-evident
C) The premise must be well-known (mashhur)
D) The premise must be a first principle

Key Takeaway
In dialectical reasoning, a premise is acceptable if it is well-known, regardless of whether it is demonstratively true.

Dialectical Topic: Privation and Possession
States that privation and possession are mutually entailing in a well-known way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mutual implication between privation and possession and use this dialectical topic to construct or evaluate an argument.

Core Idea
Privation and possession mutually imply each other — a foundational dialectical locus.

In the Islamic dialectical tradition (jadal), specific 'topics' (amākin) provide starting points for argumentation. One such topic is the pair of privation (ʿadam) and possession (milka), such as blindness and sight. This passage states that their mutual entailment is well-known, and it even suggests that the locus can be used for necessary arguments, not merely plausible ones. We will walk through how to recognize and apply this topic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pair of opposites
Look for a term denoting a possession (e.g., sight, knowledge) and its corresponding privation (e.g., blindness, ignorance). In the passage, 'sight' (al-basar) is the possession, and 'blindness' (al-ʿamā) is the privation.
The pair must be a natural opposite where the subject is expected to have the possession.
2
Confirm the direction of implication
The passage says 'the implication from both sides is well-known' — meaning if possession holds, privation does not, and vice versa. Check that the entailment is mutual, not one-way.
For example, 'if sight is good, then blindness is an absence of good' illustrates one direction.
3
Apply the topic to a concrete example
Take a real-world pair: justice/possession of justice vs. injustice/privation. If a person is just, it follows that they are not unjust. Conversely, if they are unjust, they are not just.
Try to construct an argument using a different Islamic example, such as knowledge and ignorance.
4
Evaluate the truth status
The author notes that if taken straightforwardly (ʿalā al-istiqāma), the locus might be true, meaning it can yield certain conclusions, not just dialectical probability. Consider when this might fail (e.g., when the subject is not naturally fit to possess the attribute).
This nuance allows the topic to be used in both dialectical and demonstrative contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العدم والملكة فاللزوم من الجانبين مشهور فإنه إذا كان البصر حسنا فالعمى عدم حسن؛ ويُشبه أن يكون هذا الموضع حقًا إذا أخذ على الاستقامة.
And as for privation and possession, the implication from both sides is well-known. For if sight is good, then blindness is an absence of good; and it seems that this locus is true if taken straightforwardly.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage covers the dialectical topic of privation (العدم) and possession (الملكة). Examples: sight is the possession, blindness is the privation. The relationship is that if possession is present, the privation is absent, and vice versa. The author says the implication (اللزوم) from both sides is well-known, meaning in dialectic one can argue from possession to privation or vice versa. The final remark suggests that if this topic is taken straightforwardly (على الاستقامة), it might even be true, not just dialectical, but the emphasis remains on its well-known character.

Key Terms
العدم والملكة
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are opposites with no implication
B) There is a one-way implication
C) There is a two-way implication that is well-known
D) They are identical

Key Takeaway
The mutual implication between privation and possession is a well-known dialectical topic that allows for valid arguments from the presence or absence of a quality.

Two Levels of Entailment for Relatives
Introduces two types of entailment for relatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real entailment (ملازم حقيقي) from well-known entailment (ملازم مشهور) in correlative terms and identify which level is being invoked in a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Relatives imply each other dialectically, but real entailment requires strict conditions.

When we say 'father implies son' or 'knower implies known', we assume a mutual relationship. But in rigorous logic, these entailments are not automatic. The passage introduces two levels: real entailment, which demands precise parallelism, and well-known entailment, which suffices in dialectical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative pair
Find the two terms that are correlatives in the statement, e.g., father-son, knower-known, three-multiples-three-parts.
Relatives are always paired.
2
Determine the type of entailment claimed
Check if the author says 'real' (حقيقي) or 'well-known' (مشهور). Real entailment has conditions; well-known is unconditional.
The passage says: 'ملازماً حقيقياً' vs 'ملازما مشهورًا'.
3
Check for the condition of corresponding parallelism
If the entailment is claimed as real, verify that the relation is symmetric in the appropriate way (e.g., knowledge relates to knower actively and to known passively; the parallelism must be maintained).
The passage warns that in reality, entailment requires preserving الموازاة المعادلة.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتضايفات فإنها متلازمة على الاستقامة إذا روعي ما يجب أن يراعى ملازماً حقيقياً، ومتلازمة على الإطلاق ملازما مشهورًا. فيقال إن كان ذو ثلاثة أضعاف كثير الأضعاف فذو ثلاثة أجزاء كثير الأجزاء، وإن كان العلم ظنا فالمعلوم مظنون. وأما في الحقيقة فليس يجب أن يلزم إلا بشرائط ؛ وذلك لأن العلم من حيث هو فيه مضاف إلى العالم، ومضاف من وجه آخر إلى المعلوم. وليس يجب إذا كان العلم إدراكًا أن يكون العالم مدركًا أي واقعا عليه الإدراك أو المعلوم مدرَكًا أي واقعا منه الإدراك بل يجب أن تكون الموازاة المعادلة محفوظة. فلو قلت إذا كان محسوس ما معلوما كان حس ما علما كان ذلك قولًا جدليًّا؛ إذ الحس ليس بعلم أصلا، وبالعكس.
And as for relatives (correlatives), they are mutually entailing straightforwardly if regard is given to what must be regarded as a real entailment, and they are mutually entailing absolutely as a well-known entailment. So it is said: If a thing with three parts is many-parted, then a thing with three parts is many-parted? Actually the example: 'If that which has three multiples is many-multiples, then that which has three parts is many-parts' and 'If knowledge is opinion, then the known is opined.' But in reality, it does not have to entail except under conditions. That is because knowledge, as such, is relative to the knower, and relative from another aspect to the known. And it is not necessary that if knowledge is apprehension, then the knower is apprehended (i.e., upon him the apprehension falls) or the known is apprehended (i.e., from him the apprehension originates). Rather, the corresponding parallelism must be preserved. So if you said: 'If a perceived thing is known, then a sense is knowledge', that would be a dialectical statement, because sense is not knowledge at all, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This long passage deals with relatives (المتضايفات), such as 'father and son' or 'knower and known'. The author distinguishes two levels: (1) real entailment (تلازم حقيقي) which requires conditions (e.g., the relative terms must be strictly opposite and the relation must be symmetric in reality), and (2) dialectical entailment (تلازم مشهور) which only needs to be commonly accepted. Examples are given: from 'having three multiples' to 'having three parts' (if one is many in one respect, the other is many in the corresponding respect), and from 'knowledge is opinion' to 'the known is opined'. However, the author warns that in reality, entailment is not automatic. For knowledge, it is relative to the knower and the known in different ways. It is not necessary that if knowledge is apprehension (إدراك), then the knower is apprehended (i.e., passive) or the known is apprehended (active). Rather, the relation must be correctly aligned (الموازاة المعادلة محفوظة). A dialectical example is given: 'If a perceived thing is known, then sense is knowledge' – this is only dialectical because sense is not knowledge at all. The passage highlights the difference between rigorous logical entailment and dialectical acceptability.

Key Terms
المتضايفات
ملازم حقيقي
ملازم مشهور
الموازاة المعادلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes real entailment from well-known entailment for relatives?
A) Real entailment requires the preservation of corresponding parallelism (الموازاة المعادلة), while well-known entailment does not.
B) Well-known entailment requires strict conditions, while real entailment is based on common acceptance.
C) Real entailment is always true, while well-known entailment is only dialectical.
D) There is no difference; both are the same.

Key Takeaway
Relatives entail each other dialectically without conditions, but real entailment demands strict parallel correspondence.

Conditions for Real Entailment: The Parallelism
Explains the condition of corresponding parallelism for real entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why real entailment for relatives requires preserving the corresponding parallelism (الموازاة المعادلة), using the example of knowledge.

Core Idea
Real entailment fails when the relation is misaligned: knower and known are not symmetric.

The passage warns that real entailment is not automatic. Using knowledge as an example, it shows that the relation between knower and known is complex: knowledge is relative to the knower in one way and to the known in another. The mistake is to think that if knowledge is an apprehension, then the knower is apprehended or the known is apprehended. The correct condition is to preserve the corresponding parallelism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative pair in the example
The pair is 'knowledge' (علم) relative to 'knower' (عالم) and 'known' (معلوم). Note that knowledge is related to each differently.
Knowledge is an act of the knower and an attribute related to the known.
2
Note the asymmetry in the relations
Knowledge relates to the knower as an active act (the knower apprehends), and to the known as a passive object (the known is apprehended). They are not symmetric.
The passage says: 'العلم من حيث هو فيه مضاف إلى العالم، ومضاف من وجه آخر إلى المعلوم'.
3
Check the dialetical example's failure
In the example, 'If a perceived thing is known, then sense is knowledge', the parallelism fails because 'sense' (حس) is not the same type of relation as 'knowledge' (علم). Sense is a faculty, not an act of intellection.
The passage concludes: 'إذ الحس ليس بعلم أصلا'.
4
Apply the condition of corresponding parallelism
For real entailment, the relation must be of the same type: active-active or passive-passive. Here, 'perceived thing known' is passive, but 'sense is knowledge' is active? Actually, we need to align: if the object is known, the faculty is not necessarily knowledge. The parallelism is broken.
The condition is 'أن تكون الموازاة المعادلة محفوظة'.

Source Text

وأما المتضايفات فإنها متلازمة على الاستقامة إذا روعي ما يجب أن يراعى ملازماً حقيقياً، ومتلازمة على الإطلاق ملازما مشهورًا. فيقال إن كان ذو ثلاثة أضعاف كثير الأضعاف فذو ثلاثة أجزاء كثير الأجزاء، وإن كان العلم ظنا فالمعلوم مظنون. وأما في الحقيقة فليس يجب أن يلزم إلا بشرائط ؛ وذلك لأن العلم من حيث هو فيه مضاف إلى العالم، ومضاف من وجه آخر إلى المعلوم. وليس يجب إذا كان العلم إدراكًا أن يكون العالم مدركًا أي واقعا عليه الإدراك أو المعلوم مدرَكًا أي واقعا منه الإدراك بل يجب أن تكون الموازاة المعادلة محفوظة. فلو قلت إذا كان محسوس ما معلوما كان حس ما علما كان ذلك قولًا جدليًّا؛ إذ الحس ليس بعلم أصلا، وبالعكس.
And as for relatives (correlatives), they are mutually entailing straightforwardly if regard is given to what must be regarded as a real entailment, and they are mutually entailing absolutely as a well-known entailment. So it is said: If a thing with three parts is many-parted, then a thing with three parts is many-parted? The Dialectical Example: Sense vs. Knowledge
Presents a dialectical example to illustrate false parallelism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dialectical argument involving relatives and explain why it fails as a real entailment.

Core Idea
If 'perceived thing is known' then 'sense is knowledge' is dialectical because sense is not knowledge.

The passage ends with a cautionary example: if someone says 'If a perceived thing is known, then sense is knowledge', that is a dialectical statement, not a real one. This illustrates how easily misalignment of relatives leads to false entailments. Sense (حس) is not a form of knowledge (علم), so the inference only works in dialectic where well-known entailment is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relative pairs involved
First pair: 'perceived thing' (محسوس) and 'known' (معلوم). Second pair: 'sense' (حس) and 'knowledge' (علم).
The argument tries to connect these pairs.
2
Examine the dialectical claim
The claim is: if the object of sense is known, then the faculty of sense is a type of knowledge. This is not a real entailment because sense and knowledge are distinct.
The passage says: 'إذ الحس ليس بعلم أصلا'.
3
Recognize the broken parallelism
The relation between perceived thing and known is that of object to cognition. The relation between sense and knowledge would be that of faculty to cognition. These are not parallel; one is an object, the other is a means.
The condition of الموازاة المعادلة is violated.
4
Classify the argument as dialectical
Because the entailment is accepted in common discourse (e.g., 'seeing is knowing') but not in strict logic, it is a dialectical (جدلي) statement.
The passage calls it 'قول جدلي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Analogies (al-Naza'ir) in Dialectic
Defines analogies as things with a relation to something from which a name is derived, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify analogies (al-nazair) and distinguish the two types of relations (end-to-agent and matter-to-end) in dialectical arguments.

Core Idea
Analogies are not mere similarities; they are name-deriving relations.

In dialectical debates, one often appeals to analogies to strengthen or weaken an argument. But what exactly counts as an analogy in Islamic logic? This passage defines analogies as things that derive their names from a relation to something else, and it introduces two types of such relations. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze and use analogical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of analogies
Find the phrase that defines analogies in the Arabic: 'النظائر الأمور التي لها نسبة إلى الشيء فيشتق لها منه اسم'. This sets the foundation.
2
Examine the example: 'just' from 'justice'
Notice how the name 'just' (عدل) is derived from 'justice' (عدالة). This illustrates the core idea of name derived from a relation.
3
Distinguish the first type: end-to-agent
The text says 'النسبة قد تكون للغاية إلى الفاعل' (the relation may be from the end to the agent). Example: 'healthful treatment' – the treatment (agent) is named after health (end).
Think of the agent as the cause and the end as the purpose.
4
Distinguish the second type: matter-to-end
The text says 'وقد تكون للمادة إلى الغاية' (and it may be from the matter to the end). Example: 'putrid disease' – the disease (end) is named after putrefaction (matter).
Here the matter is the material cause, and the end is the state or condition.
5
Apply the concept to a new dialectical context
Think of another example from Islamic jurisprudence: 'intoxicating' (muski) is derived from 'intoxication' (sukr). Which type of relation is this? (Answer: end-to-agent).
This helps you recognize analogies in actual dialectical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ من المواضع الخارجة مواضع كانت تعرف بالنظائر ؛ ويعني بالنظائر الأمور التي لها نسبة إلى الشيء فيشتق لها منه اسم كالعدل الذي هو نظير العدالة والنسبة قد تكون للغاية إلى الفاعل فيقال علاج صحي، وقد تكون للمادة إلى الغاية ومشتق منها اسم فيقال مثلا مرض عفوني.
Then from the external topics are topics known by analogies (al-naza'ir). And by analogies he means things that have a relation to something from which a name is derived for them, like 'just' which is analogous to 'justice'. And the relation may be from the end to the agent, so it is said 'healthful treatment', and it may be from the matter to the end, and a name derived from it, so it is said, for example, 'putrid disease'.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage introduces a type of external dialectical topic called 'analogies' (النظائر). The term 'nazir' (plural naza'ir) refers to a thing that has a relation to another thing such that its name is derived from that other thing. Example: 'just (عدل)' is derived from 'justice (عدالة)'. Two types of relations are distinguished: (1) from the end (الغاية) to the agent (الفاعل), e.g., 'healthful treatment' where the treatment has a relation to health (the end), so the name is derived from health. (2) from the matter (المادة) to the end, e.g., 'putrid disease' where the disease's material cause (putrefaction) gives the name. These analogies are used in dialectic to establish or refute arguments by appealing to the derived relationship.

Key Terms
النظائر
النسبة
الغاية
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Matter to end
C) End to agent
D) Substance to accident

Key Takeaway
Analogies in dialectic are name-deriving relations, not mere similarity; they come in two forms: from end to agent or from matter to end.
