Evaluating Derived Forms in Dialectical Arguments
Discusses derived forms as topics where something is said to be 'in the manner of' something else, like 'habitual course'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify statements of derived forms (al-taṣārīf) in dialectical arguments and evaluate whether they correctly represent an effect emanating from a cause.

Core Idea
Derived forms must be genuine effects emanating from a cause; not every expression of procession qualifies.

In jadal, one common external topic is 'derived forms'—where we say something is 'in the course of nature' or 'habitual course'. The author introduces this topic but immediately raises a doubt: are such expressions truly derived forms? You will learn to spot these statements and apply the author's criterion to evaluate them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed derived form
Find the statement in the argument that uses a phrase like 'this is in the course of nature' or 'it is customary'. This is the candidate for a derived form.
In the passage, the example is 'جار مجرى الطبيعة' (in the course of nature).
2
Determine the alleged source and effect
Identify what is said to be the source (asl) and what is the derived effect (far'). For example, recovery (الوضع) is said to emanate from sickness (المرض).
The source must be something that produces the effect.
3
Check for causal emanation
Analyze whether the relationship is genuinely causal: does the source necessarily produce the effect? If the effect is merely metaphorically associated, it may not be a valid derived form.
The author expresses doubt that 'course of nature' always represents such a causal relation.
4
Apply the author's critique
If the relation is not causal, the argument using that derived form may be weak in jadal. The opponent can object that the statement is not truly a derived form.
This step trains you to spot where an interlocutor might raise doubt (نظر).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المواضع أيضًا ما يكون مأخوذاً من التصاريف كما يقال هذا هو جار مجرى الطبيعة؛ وهذا مأخذ الحكمة فتكون نسبة الجاري مجرى الطبيعة نسبة ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف: وفيه نظر فإن ما يكون على سبيل التصاريف فذلك ما يكون صادرًا عن الشيء كالوضع عن المرض.
And from the topics also is what is taken from derived forms, as it is said: 'This is in the course of nature.' And this is a principle of wisdom, so the relation of 'in the course of nature' is a relation of what is on the way of derived forms. But there is doubt: for what is on the way of derived forms is that which emanates from a thing, like recovery from sickness.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage covers another external topic: derived forms (التصاريف). The author explains that statements like 'this is in the course of nature' (جار مجرى الطبيعة) belong to this topic. The phrase is said to be a 'principle of wisdom' (مأخذ الحكمة). The relation expressed by 'in the course of nature' is a relation of proceeding from something, similar to how recovery (الوضع) proceeds from sickness. However, the author notes a problem (وفيه نظر): in derived forms, the thing should be an effect emanating from its cause (e.g., recovery from sickness), but the phrase 'in the course of nature' might not always represent such a procession correctly. This is a critical note, typical of a teacher refining the definition.

Key Terms
التصاريف
جار مجرى الطبيعة
صادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author question about derived forms?
A) They are always useful
B) The example 'course of nature' is not a proper derived form
C) They are only for ethics
D) They are synonymous with analogies

Key Takeaway
Derived forms in jadal require a genuine causal relationship between the source and the effect; not all expressions of procession qualify.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Applying Dialectical Topics in Ethical Science
Shows how dialectical topics apply in ethics with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze a dialectical inference in ethical science using the relationship between a quality and its possessor.

Core Idea
Dialectical topics provide probabilistic premises, not certain demonstrations.

This lesson shows how the dialectical topics discussed earlier can be put to work in the practical science of ethics. The author gives a concrete example: if justice is praised, then the just person is praised. This inference may seem straightforward, but the author cautions that it is not always certain—it is a dialectical move, not a demonstrative one. Understanding this distinction is key to using these topics correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example's components
The example uses two terms: 'justice' (العدالة) as an abstract virtue, and 'the just person' (العدل) as its possessor. Notice the relationship: a quality and the entity that possesses it.
2
Analyze the logical form
The inference is a conditional: 'If justice is praised, then the just person is praised.' This is based on the correlation between a quality and its subject. In dialectic, such correlations are accepted as plausible.
3
Distinguish dialectical from demonstrative
The author adds 'وقد لا تكون' (it may not be the case), indicating the inference is not universally valid. In demonstration, we require necessity; dialectic only requires plausibility or general acceptance.
4
Apply to ethical reasoning
In ethical discussions, such inferences can be used as premises for persuasion, but one must be aware of potential counterexamples. For instance, a just person might not be praised if enemies are judging. This highlights the dialectical nature.

Source Text

Then the benefit of these topics may be in a specific manner for ethical science, as it is said: 'If justice is praised, then the just person is praised' – and it may not be [the case].
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This short passage notes that the external dialectical topics can be applied specifically to ethical science (العلم الخلقي). An example is given: 'If justice (العدالة) is praised, then the just person (العدل) is praised.' This follows the pattern of analogies or derived forms, where a quality and its possessor are taken as relatives. However, the author adds 'and it may not be the case' (وقد لا تكون), indicating that this implication is not always valid in demonstration; it is only dialectically acceptable. This suggests that the benefit of these topics depends on the context.

Understanding Topics of Resemblance as Dialectical Analogies
Describes topics of resemblance as analogies where one part is made antecedent and the other consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a topic of resemblance (mutashabih) and distinguish it from demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
Topics of resemblance are analogies used in dialectic, not demonstration.

In this lesson, we explore the topic of resemblance, a dialectical tool where analogies are drawn between two things based on similarity. Unlike demonstrative arguments, these yield no certainty, only persuasion. The passage illustrates this with an example from the relationship between science and opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the key phrase 'وهذا هو جدلي صرف' (and this is pure dialectic).
This verdict is central to understanding the epistemic status.
2
Identify the analogical structure
The example has the form 'If A then B' based on resemblance between science and opinion. Locate the antecedent and consequent.
The resemblance is between 'one science of opposites' and 'one opinion of opposites'.
3
Recognize the dialectical nature
The author states it is 'pure dialectic' (jadaliy sirf), meaning it does not produce certainty, only persuasion.
This distinguishes it from burhani (demonstrative) arguments.
4
Compare to demonstrative reasoning
Consider why this argument is not demonstrative: the resemblance does not guarantee a necessary connection. In demonstration, the middle term is a cause.
Here, the similarity is superficial, not rooted in a shared 'illah (effective cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي من المواضع مواضع المتشابهات وهي كأنها تمثيلات يجعل البعض منها مقدما والآخر تاليا؛ وهذا هو جدلي صرف كقولهم إن كان قد يكون بالأضداد علم واحد فقد يكون بالأضداد ظن واحد.
And among them – i.e., among the topics – are the topics of resemblances, and they are like analogies where some are made antecedent and others consequent. And this is pure dialectic, like their saying: 'If it is possible that there is one science of opposites, then it is possible that there is one opinion of opposites.'
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage introduces the 'topics of resemblances' (مواضع المتشابهات). These are analogical patterns (تمثيلات) where one part is taken as the antecedent (مقدم) and another as the consequent (تالي) based on similarity. The author emphasizes that this is pure dialectic (جدلي صرف), meaning it has no demonstrative force. Example: 'If it is possible that there is one science that knows opposites, then it is possible that there is one opinion that holds opposites.' The argument is based on resemblance between science and opinion. This is a standard dialectical move, often used in jadal.

Key Terms

جدلي صرف

Using Unity and Plurality for Refutation
Describes the topic of unity and plurality as useful for refutation, with a demonstrative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a demonstrative refutation by applying the topic of unity and plurality to a theological or legal claim.

Core Idea
Unity and plurality can turn a dialectical exchange into a certain refutation.

The author has just surveyed several dialectical topics that are useful for argumentation. Now he introduces one that is more powerful: the topic of unity and plurality. Not only can it refute an opponent's claim, but it does so with certainty. This lesson unpacks how that works through a concrete example from the science of logic and then applies it to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be refuted
Start with the opponent's universal claim, e.g., 'All knowledge is understanding.' This is the position you intend to refute.
The claim should be a universal affirmative (every A is B) for the topic to apply directly.
2
Find a property of the subject that involves plurality
Consider the subject 'knowledge'. It can be of many things simultaneously (e.g., knowledge of the world includes countless facts at once). This is a plurality property.
The property should be undeniable or accepted by both sides.
3
Show the predicate lacks that plurality property
Now consider the predicate 'understanding'. Understanding (as a mental act) cannot grasp many things at once — it is limited to one thing at a time. So understanding is characterized by unity in its object, not plurality.
If the predicate cannot share the plurality property, it cannot be identical to the subject.
4
Conclude that the claim is false demonstratively
Since the subject has a property (plurality) that the predicate lacks, the universal claim is necessarily false. This refutation is demonstrative because it relies on the nature of the things themselves, not on common opinion.
The author explicitly marks this as burhānī (demonstrative), meaning it yields certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها مواضع النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة؛ وهذا يصلح للإبطال كمن يضع أن كل علم فهم فيقال إن العلم قد يكون بأشياء كثيرة معا. والفهم لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك ؛ وهذا الموضع برهاني.
And among them are the topics of relation to unity and plurality. And this is suitable for refutation, as when someone posits that every knowledge is understanding. So it is said: 'Knowledge may be of many things at once, but understanding cannot be like that.' And this topic is demonstrative.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage covers the topic of unity and plurality (النسبة إلى الوحدة والكثرة). The author notes it is particularly useful for refutation (الإبطال). An example is given: if someone claims that all knowledge (علم) is understanding (فهم), one can refute it by noting that knowledge can encompass many things simultaneously (e.g., the entire world), whereas understanding (as a mental act) cannot grasp many things at once. The author then adds an important remark: this topic is demonstrative (برهاني), meaning it can produce certainty, unlike the previous dialectical topics. Thus, this locus bridges dialectic and demonstration.

Key Terms
الوحدة والكثرة
الإبطال
برهاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the topic of unity and plurality considered demonstrative?
A) It uses well-known opinions accepted by the opponent
B) It refutes by showing a logical difference in capacity between the subject and predicate
C) It is based on a revealed text
D) It relies on analogical extension from one case to another

Key Takeaway
The topic of unity and plurality provides a demonstrative refutation by showing that a property involving plurality holds for the subject but not for the predicate, thereby disproving a universal claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Use the topic of unity/plurality to refute the claim: 'Every act of worship requires intention (niyyah).' Consider that acts of worship are many (prayer, fasting, charity) and some (like removing harm from the road) do not require intention — thus the universal claim is false.

The Four Types of More-and-Less in Dialectical Reasoning
Explains four types of more-and-less reasoning with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of more-and-less reasoning and apply them to dialectical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
More-and-less reasoning is a dialectical tool that uses comparative degrees to prove or refute claims.

In dialectical debates, disputants often argue by comparing degrees of intensity or quantity. The logicians identified four distinct argument patterns based on 'more and less' (الأقل والأكثر). Each pattern follows a specific rule about how predicates or subjects relate across degrees. Mastering these patterns sharpens your ability to construct and critique arguments in theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the comparative dimension
Determine what is being compared—predicate intensity, subject quantity, or a combination. This orients you to the type.
The four types differ in which element (subject or predicate) is being varied.
2
Understand the first type (predicate proportion)
When the subject has more of a quality, the predicate increases proportionally. Example: if pleasure is good, more pleasure is more good.
This is a direct proportionality between the degree of subject and predicate.
3
Analyze the second type (worthiness)
Compare two predicates based on 'more worthy' (أولى). Absence of the more worthy refutes the less worthy; presence of the less worthy proves the more worthy.
The meaning of 'more worthy' varies across sciences, so careful definition is required.
4
Apply the third type (subject multiplicity)
Reverse of the second: plurality is in the subjects, with a single predicate. If the predicate does not apply to the more worthy subject, it does not apply to the less worthy; but if it applies to the less worthy, it also applies to the more worthy.
This is used when the same predicate is considered for multiple subjects.
5
Examine the fourth type (cross comparison)
Two predicates and two subjects, where neither predicate is more worthy for its subject than the other is for its subject. This type sets up a balanced comparison.
This type is less common but useful for symmetrical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها مواضع مأخوذة من الأقل والأكثر وهي أربعة: أحدها أن يجعل هو أكثر في معنى الموضوع أكثر في معنى المحمول كما يقال إن كانت اللذة خيرا فما هو أكثر لذة فهو أكثر خيرًا. والثاني أن يعتبر مع المحمول محمول آخر حكم في أن يحمل أولى من حكم هذا؛ فإن لم يحمل ولم يؤخذ ذلك ففي المشهور أن هذا لا يحمل ولا يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإبطال. وإن وجد ما ليس أولى ففي المشهور أن الأولى يؤخذ؛ وهذا للإثبات. وحال هذا الموضع في العلوم يختلف بحسب اختلاف المفهوم من لفظة «أولى». والثالث عكس ما هذا وهو أن تكون الكثرة في جهة الموضوعين، والمحمول واحد منتظر إن لم يوجد المحمول لما هو أولى أن يوجد له لم يوجد لما ليس أولى، وإن وجد لما ليس أولى فقد وجد لما هو أولى. والرابع هو أن يكون الاعتبار بین محمولین و بین موضوعين، وأحد المحمولين ليس أولى لموضوعه من المحمول الآخر بموضوعه.
And among them are topics taken from more and less, and they are four. First: to make what is more in the meaning of the subject more in the meaning of the predicate, as it is said: 'If pleasure is good, then what is more pleasurable is more good.' Second: to consider with the predicate another predicate that is more worthy to be predicated than the ruling of this [predicate]; if it is not predicated and that is not taken, then in the well-known [opinion] it is not predicated and not taken; this is for refutation. And if there exists what is not more worthy, then in the well-known [opinion] the more worthy is taken; this is for proof. The status of this topic in the sciences differs according to the understanding of the word 'more worthy'. Third: the opposite of this, which is that the plurality is on the side of the two subjects, and the predicate is one, awaiting: if the predicate is not found for what is more worthy to have it, it is not found for what is not more worthy; and if it is found for what is not more worthy, then it has been found for what is more worthy. Fourth: that the consideration is between two predicates and two subjects, and one of the two predicates is not more worthy for its subject than the other predicate for its subject.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage lists four types of dialectical topics based on 'more and less' (الأقل والأكثر). 

**First type**: If the subject has more of a quality, then the predicate should also be more. Example: If pleasure is good, then more pleasurable things are more good. This is an argument by proportion. 

**Second type**: Involves comparing two predicates where one is more worthy (أولى) of being predicated than the other. If a more worthy predicate is not found, then the less worthy is also not predicated (refutation). Conversely, if a less worthy predicate is found, then the more worthy is also predicated (proof). The author notes that the meaning of 'more worthy' varies across sciences. 

**Third type**: The reverse of the second: the plurality (i.e., the greater quantity) is in the subjects, while the predicate is one. The rule: if the predicate does not apply to what is more worthy of it, it does not apply to what is less worthy; but if it applies to what is less worthy, it also applies to what is more worthy. 

**Fourth type**: The consideration is between two predicates and two subjects, where neither predicate is more worthy for its subject than the other is for its subject. 

These four types are used in dialectical debates to argue by degrees of intensity or quantity. The examples show how such reasoning operates in hypothetical cases.

Key Terms
الأقل والأكثر
أولى
الإبطال
الإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the function of the second type when a more worthy predicate exists?
A) Proof
B) Refutation
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
The four types of more-and-less reasoning provide structured ways to argue from comparative degrees, varying by whether the comparison focuses on predicates, subjects, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following claim: 'If justice is good, then more justice is better.' Identify which type of more-and-less reasoning this exemplifies and explain the rule.
?
In a debate on whether God's mercy or wrath is more predominant, a disputant argues: 'If mercy is not found for the righteous (the more worthy), then it is not found for the sinners (the less worthy).' Is this a valid application of the third type? Why or why not?

Dialectical Places Derived from Equality
Author lists four dialectical places derived from equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of equality-based dialectical places and classify them as dialectical rather than scientific.

Core Idea
Dialectical places from equality are argumentative patterns, not demonstrations.

This passage introduces a set of argumentative tools known as 'places' (مواضع) derived from the concept of equality (مساواة). Unlike scientific demonstrations, these places are suitable only for dialectical disputation. The author enumerates four specific forms and illustrates their use with an example from fiqh (wine) and everyday reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four forms of equality-based places
The author lists four: (1) two coextensive predicates in first and non-first subject, (2) two subjects with one predicate, (3) two predicates for two subjects, (4) predicate applied in sense of more and less absolutely. Locate them in the Arabic passage.
These correspond to combinations of subjects and predicates.
2
Analyze the wine example
The example uses 'more and less' intoxicating wine to show that absolute predication remains. This is a dialectical move, not a proof.
The predicate 'intoxicating' is applied absolutely regardless of degree.
3
Distinguish scientific from dialectical
The author explicitly states that none of these places are scientific (علمي). This means they cannot yield certainty, only persuade in debate.
Scientific places (برهانية) produce certain knowledge; dialectical places (جدلية) only produce probable or persuasive conclusions.
4
Recognize limitations and opposition
Many things (like existence, possibility) do not admit of degrees. These places have only dialectical opposition (عناد جدلي), not scientific refutation.
Dialectical opposition uses counterexamples that show the place does not hold universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هنا مواضع مأخوذة من المساواة بعدد هذه المواضع بأعيانها وهي أربعة أيضًا حال محمولين متساويين في الأولى وغير الأولى في الموضوع أو موضوعين بمحمول أو محمولين لموضوعين. ثمَّ يعتبر ذلك بعينه؛ وليس شيء منها علميا . ومن المواضع أيضًا ما إذا قيل المحمول على موضوع أو على شيء بمعنى الأولى والأكثر ؛ فهو مقول عليه بالإطلاق مثاله إن كان خمرًا أقل إسكارًا من خمر وأكثر فهو مسكر على الإطلاق وهذا ليس يصلح للإبطال فإنه ليس إذا لم يقل بأقل وأكثر لم يقل بإطلاق. وكثير من الأشياء لا يقبل الأقل والأكثر؛ ومثل هذا الموضع ليس له عناد علمي بل له عناد جدلي. وقريب من هذا ما إذا كان الشيء ممكناً أو موجوداً أو كذا وكذا في حال من الأحوال فهو أيضًا بتلك الحال على الإطلاق. فإن ما ليس بممكن في نفسه فليس بممكن في حال؛ وهذا أيضًا للإثبات. ويعاند هذا الموضع بأمثلة مشهورة؛ قد يوجد الشيء صالحًا في وقت؛ وليس صالحًا على الإطلاق، ويوجد الشيء غير فاسد في وقت؛ وليس غير فاسد على الإطلاق. وعلى هذا فإن المواضع كثيرة ولكل واحد منها نظائر كثيرة تعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then here are places taken from equality, in the number of these same places, and they are four also: the case of two coextensive predicates in the first and non-first in the subject, or two subjects with one predicate, or two predicates for two subjects. Then that is considered exactly; and none of them is scientific. And among the places also is when it is said that the predicate [applies] to a subject or to a thing in the sense of 'more and less'; it is predicated of it absolutely. For example, if a wine is less intoxicating than another wine and more [than a third], it is intoxicating absolutely. And this is not suitable for refutation, because if it is not said with more and less, it is not said absolutely. And many things do not admit of more and less; and such a place has no scientific opposition but only dialectical opposition. And close to this is when a thing is possible or existent or such-and-such in a particular state; it is also in that state absolutely. For what is not possible in itself is not possible in a state; and this also is for affirmation. And this place is opposed with well-known examples: sometimes a thing is beneficial at a time and not beneficial absolutely, and a thing is not corrupt at a time and not not-corrupt absolutely. And so the places are many, and each has many analogues known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage discusses a category of dialectical 'places' (مواضع) derived from the concept of equality (مساواة). The author identifies four specific forms: (1) two coextensive predicates applied to a subject in first and non-first sense, (2) two subjects with one predicate, (3) two predicates for two subjects, (4) the predicate applied in the sense of 'more and less' absolutely. He emphasizes that none of these are suitable for scientific (برهاني) demonstration; they are purely dialectical. The example of wine (خمر) illustrates the 'more and less' place: if one wine is less intoxicating than another but more than a third, it is still intoxicating absolutely. This cannot be used to refute the claim that all wine is intoxicating, because the predicate 'intoxicating' is applied absolutely, not comparatively. The author then notes that many things (like existence, possibility) do not admit of degrees, and such places are opposed dialectically using well-known counterexamples (e.g., something beneficial at one time but not absolutely). The passage underscores the limited scope of these topics—they are tools for debate, not certain proof.

Key Terms
موضع (mawḍiʿ): A dialectical 'place' or topic—a standard argumentative pattern used in disputation. [Note: In Aristotelian logic, 'topos' is a general argumentative schema; in Islamic dialectic (jadal), it is a defined procedure for generating or refuting claims.]
مساواة (musāwāh): Equality—here refers to the relation between coextensive predicates or terms that are mutually applicable. [Note: Stable across logical and dialectical texts.]
عناد (ʿinād): Opposition or rebuttal—specifically, the dialectical counter-argument that challenges a place. [Note: In later jadal terminology, 'muʿānada' is a specific type of objection.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the example of wine (one wine less intoxicating than another yet still intoxicating absolutely) not suitable for refutation (إبطال)?
A) Because intoxicating does not admit of more and less.
B) Because the predicate 'intoxicating' is applied absolutely, not comparatively.
C) Because the example is not a real case.
D) Because it is only useful for affirmation.

Key Takeaway
The equality-based dialectical places are useful for debate but do not yield certain knowledge; they are tools for persuasion, not demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

(Answer: two predicates for two subjects, or coextensive predicates? The statement uses one predicate 'obligatory' for two subjects, so it matches the second form: two subjects with one predicate.)

Distinguishing Athan and Afdal
Author defines and classifies the concepts of 'more effective' and 'better' in dialectical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'athan' (more effective) and 'afdal' (better) using the author's example of knowledge vs. clothing.

Core Idea
More effective is not always better; better is not always more effective.

The author opens a new chapter on comparative terms used in dialectic. He first distinguishes between 'more effective' and 'better', showing that they are conceptually distinct and not interchangeable. A clear example is given: knowledge is better than clothing, but not more effective for a naked person. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
Find 'athan' and 'afdal' in the passage. Note how they are introduced and that the author emphasizes their concepts are different.
The Arabic phrases are: المفهوم من الأثر غير المفهوم من الأفضل
2
Extract the defining difference
The author says the concept of athan is different from afdal. Locate the sentence: 'ثمَّ المفهوم من الأثر غير المفهوم من الأفضل'. What distinguishes them?
3
Analyze the example of knowledge vs. clothing
Why is knowledge better but not more effective? What is the criterion for effectiveness? For betterness? The author explicitly states knowledge is better (أفضل) but not more effective (أثر) for a naked person.
Clothing is effective in covering, knowledge is not.
4
Test the distinction with another example from the passage
Later, the author discusses essential vs. accidental virtues and says what is effective essentially is better. Can you find an example of something that is more effective but not better?
Consider a powerful medicine that harms; the author does not give such an example, but you can infer.

فصل: في الأولى والأثر
ويلي المواضع التي مرَّ ذكرها المواضع التي بعد نحو الآثر والأفضل؛ فظاهر الحال من البحث عن الآثر والأفضل يقتضي أن يكون متعلقا بالأمور الخلقية. وما هو أولى بالإيثار والاجتناب فقط لكن حقيقة النظر فيهما مقتضية للنظر في الأولى والأخرى، وفي الأزيد والأنقص؛ وذلك قد يتعدى الأمور الخلقية. وأما مشاركة هذا البحث عن الأولى والأزيد وما ليس بأولى وأزيد فيتبين لك من جهة أن جميع ما ينفع في الأزيد والأنقص ينفع في الآثر والأفضل، وأكثر ما ينفع في الآثر والأفضل ينفع فيهما. وإن كان ليس كله كذلك على ما ظنه بعض المتكلمين في هذا الفن؛ وذلك لأن كثيرًا من المواضع المعدة نحو الآثر والأفضل لا ينفع في الأزيد والأنقص مثل الموضع المشهور أن كل ما هو أطول زمانًا فهو آثر ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك. ثمَّ المفهوم من الأثر غير المفهوم من الأفضل؛ إذ الشيء قد يكون أفضل ولا يكون آثر نحو العلم فإنه أفضل وليس آثر من اللباس عند العريان. وإذا قيل أفضل فقد يقال ذلك عند التحصيل على وجوه؛ فيقال أفضل مشاركين في نوع من الفضيلة يقبل الزيادة والنقصان. ويكون لأحدهما جميع ما للآخر وزيادة، ويقال أفضل للمتشاركين في نوع من الفضيلة يقبل الأشد والأضعف، ويقال أفضل إذا كان يشاركه في نوع الفضيلة؛ وذلك النوع إما أن لا يقبل التفاوت ، وإما أن يقبل (١) لكن لهما منه على السواء. / ولأحدهما فضيلة أخرى كما إذا كان أحدهما شجاعًا عفيفًا، والآخر شجاعًا [٣٣١,] لا عفيفًا، ويقال أفضل إذا كان بينه وبين الآخر مشاركة في جنس الفضيلة إلا أن النوع الذي له دون نوع الآخر. فلا يقال إنه أفضل؛ إذ هو دور لكن يقال إنه أبهى والآخر ليس بأبهى فهو أفضل بأبهى أن تكون باقية نحو الحكمة أو نافعة فيما هو مطلوب لذاته. ومن هذا القبيل ما يحكم بأن الحكمة أفضل من الثروة. وعلى هذا فإن ما يكون بالذات من الفضائل فهو أفضل بالنسبة إلى ما يكون بالعرض. وكذلك ما يكون مؤثرًا في الشيء بذاته فإنه أفضل مما لا يكون مؤثرًا فيه بذاته بل بالعرض. وكذلك النافع في جميع الأحوال أفضل من النافع في حال دون حال، وما لا يفتقر في التحقق من المقاصد إلى الشرائط فهو أولى وأفضل مما هو مفتقر إليها .
واعلم بأن اعتبار الأفضل والأولى قد يقع في كل مقولة فيقال إن الصورة أحق بالجوهرية من الهيولى، والجزئي أولى بالوجود من الكلي. وأما في الكم
(١) النسخة قبل. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
Chapter: On the Prior and the More Effective
And following the places that have been mentioned, the places that are according to the direction of the more effective and the better: so the apparent state of the investigation into the more effective and the better necessitates that it concerns ethical matters, and what is more deserving of preference and avoidance only. But the true consideration of them necessitates consideration of the prior and the other, and of the more and the less; and that may extend beyond ethical matters. As for the sharing of this investigation of the more prior and the more [in quantity] and what is not more prior and more, it becomes clear to you from the fact that all that is useful in the more and the less is useful in the more effective and the better, and most of what is useful in the more effective and the better is useful in them. But it is not all so, as some theologians in this art thought; because many of the places prepared for the more effective and the better are not useful in the more and the less, such as the well-known place that everything that is longer in time is more effective, and it does not necessarily follow that it is so. Then the concept of the more effective is different from the concept of the better; for a thing may be better and not more effective, like knowledge: it is better and not more effective than clothing for the naked. And when 'better' is said, it may be said in realization in several ways: it is said 'better' for two sharing in a type of virtue that admits of increase and decrease, and one has all that the other has and more; and it is said 'better' for two sharing in a type of virtue that admits of stronger and weaker; and it is said 'better' if it shares with it in a type of virtue, and that type either does not admit of difference or admits [of difference?] but they have it equally. And for one of them another virtue, as when one is courageous and chaste, and the other courageous not chaste. And it is said 'better' if between it and the other there is sharing in the genus of virtue except that the species it has is inferior to the other's species. Then it is not said that it is better, because it is circular, but it is said that it is more magnificent and the other is not more magnificent, so it is better with a magnificence that is lasting like wisdom or beneficial in what is desired for itself. And of this kind is the judgment that wisdom is better than wealth. And so, what is essentially of the virtues is better relative to what is accidentally. Likewise, what is effective in a thing essentially is better than what is not effective in it essentially but accidentally. Likewise, what is beneficial in all circumstances is better than what is beneficial in some circumstances, and what does not need conditions for the realization of goals is more prior and better than what needs them. And know that the consideration of the better and the more prior may occur in every category: it is said that form is more deserving of substantiality than matter, and the particular is more prior in existence than the universal. As for quantity...
(1) The copy [reads] 'before'. What we have established is correct, as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a new chapter titled 'On the Prior and the More Effective' (في الأولى والأثر). The author introduces a set of dialectical places related to comparative terms: 'more effective' (أثر) and 'better' (أفضل), which are primarily concerned with ethical matters but can extend beyond. He first relates these to the previously discussed 'more and less' (أزيد وأنقص) places, noting that while most tools for the latter apply to the former, not all do—for example, 'longer in time' implies 'more effective' but not necessarily. A central point is the distinction between 'more effective' (أثر) and 'better' (أفضل): knowledge is better than clothing for a naked person but not more effective (since it does not cover nakedness). The author then provides a detailed classification of ways in which 'better' is predicated: (1) two sharing a virtue that admits of degrees, one having all the other has and more; (2) two sharing a virtue that admits of stronger and weaker; (3) two sharing a virtue that does not admit of difference or admits but equally, with one having an additional virtue; (4) two sharing a genus of virtue but different species, where superiority is asserted by a separate attribute. He also discusses essential vs. accidental virtues, and notes that what is beneficial in all circumstances is better than what is beneficial in some. The chapter concludes by noting that these comparisons apply across all categories, giving examples from metaphysics (form vs. matter, particular vs. universal).

Key Terms
أثر
أفضل
أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is knowledge said to be better (أفضل) than clothing, but not more effective (أثر)?
A) Because knowledge lasts longer than clothing.
B) Because knowledge is not effective in covering nakedness, which is the purpose of clothing.
C) Because clothing is not a virtue.
D) Because the better is always more effective in the long run.

Key Takeaway
Athan and afdal are distinct comparative terms: effectiveness and virtue are not the same.

The Ways of Predicating 'Better' (Afdal)
Author defines and classifies the concepts of 'more effective' and 'better' in dialectical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the four ways in which the term 'better' (afdal) is predicated according to the author.

Core Idea
'Better' can mean different things: having more of the same virtue, having a stronger degree, having an additional virtue, or being superior in a different species.

After distinguishing athan from afdal, the author delves into the various senses in which 'better' is used. He identifies several ways: two things sharing a virtue with one having all that and more, or one having a stronger degree, or one having an extra virtue, or one belonging to a superior species. This classification is crucial for precise dialectical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the classification passage
Find where the author says 'وإذا قيل أفضل فقد يقال ذلك عند التحصيل على وجوه'. This introduces the ways.
2
Extract each way
List the distinct cases the author describes. There are at least four: (1) one has all the virtue of the other and more; (2) one has a stronger degree of the same virtue; (3) one has an additional virtue alongside a shared virtue; (4) they share a genus of virtue but the other has a superior species.
The author also mentions a case where virtue does not admit of difference but they have it equally; that is not a way to say 'better'.
3
Interpret each case with an example from the text
For each way, find the example provided (e.g., one has all virtue and more; one is stronger; one has additional virtue like courage and chastity vs. courage only; one has a different species like wisdom vs. wealth).
4
Note the circularity warning
The author warns against saying one is better just because it shares a genus but has an inferior species; that leads to circular reasoning. Understand why this is a fallacy.
The phrase: 'فلا يقال إنه أفضل؛ إذ هو دور'

The Fallacy of Making an Accident a Genus
Analyzes an erroneous genus assignment using the wind example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of treating an accidental property as a genus for its subject and refute it using the criterion of numerical identity across contradictories.

Core Idea
An accidental property cannot be a genus because the subject remains numerically one while losing that property.

This passage from al-Mantiq al-Kabir presents a dialectical topic (mawḍi‘) that exposes a common logical error: mistakenly assigning an accidental property (passion) as the genus of the substance (affected). Using the concrete example of wind and air, the author demonstrates how such an inversion leads to absurdity and provides a powerful refutation technique. Mastering this topic equips you to avoid and expose definitional errors in theological and jurisprudential debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous genus assignment
Locate in the passage the claim that someone might make 'wind' a genus for 'moving air', i.e., treat an accident (movement) as the genus of the substance (air). Notice that the error is making the passion (infi‘āl) a genus for the affected (munfa‘il).
The example: 'wind is moving air' but then the arguer mistakenly insists wind is movement itself, not a moving substance.
2
Extract the refutation principle
The passage argues that a true genus must be constantly predicable of its species; the subject must retain the genus when the accidental property changes. Here, air remains air whether it is wind (moving) or not, so wind cannot be its genus. This is the core logical criterion.
The key phrase: 'air remains one in number as wind and non-wind'.
3
Apply the criterion of numerical identity
Check whether the subject (air) remains numerically identical when the alleged genus (wind) is present and absent. Since air is the same air whether moving or still, wind is an accident, not a genus. This violates the unity of genus requirement.
A genus must be one in number across all individuals of the species; an accident fluctuates, so it cannot serve as genus.
4
Generalize the dialectical topic
Understand that this is a standard refutative locus (mawḍi‘) in dialectic: if an opponent makes an accident a genus, you can refute by showing the subject persists without the accident, thus the accident is not a genus. This topic is useful in any debate involving definitions.
This topic is listed among the loci in Aristotle's Topics and later Islamic dialectical works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد ما لم يكن؛ وطبيعة الجنس واحدة بعينها بالعدد فلا يمكن أن يوجد في الأمرين المختلفين ذاتا أعني المائت وغير المائت. ومن المواضع أيضًا ما يكون على عكس هذا وهو أن يجعل الانفعال جنسًا للمنفعل؛ وقد كان مثاله في التعليم هو أنه من جعل الريح هواء متحركا ويوهم أن منعه من جهة أن الريح لا يكون هواء متحركا بل هو حركة؛ فظاهر الحال فيه يوهم أن الهواء لا يجب أن يجعل جنسًا للريح. وإذا أخذ هذا على هذا الظاهر لم يكن الانفعال جعل جنسًا للمنفعل بل المنفعل جعل جنسًا للانفعال فسببه أن يكون معناه أن يجعل الريح جنسًا للهواء المتحرك حتى يكون الهواء نوعا من الريح. وتفسير المثال مما يوضحه فإنه قيل. ولا يجوز أن يكون الهواء ريحًا أصلا. وذلك لأن الهواء يبقى واحدًا بالعدد ريحًا وغير ريح والنوع لا يبقى شخصه واحدًا بالعدد، ويخرج من جنسه إلى جنس آخر. فسببه أن يكون الريح جعل في المثال جنسًا للهواء المتحرّك؛ وإنما هو في الحقيقة عرض في الهواء لأنه حركة فيه أو لأنه متحرك منه أي من الهواء فكأنه قال يجب أن يكون الريح ليس هواء متحركا بل متحرّكا هو هواء حتى يكون جنس الريح المتحرّك وفصله أنه من هواء. ثم إن كان قول القائل إن الهواء نوع من الريح يبطل بوجوه أخرى من أن الهواء كيف يكون نوعا من الريح؛ والريح لا يوجد غير هواء: فليس ذلك مانعا أن يبطل أيضًا من هذا الوجه. وليست هذه المواضع الإبطالية بعدة نحو الأشياء التي لا يوجد لإبطالها موضع غيرها بل أن يكون أحد المواضع هي . فلو قال قائل إن الهواء نوع من الريح فإنه قد يبطل بأن يقال إن الهواء يبقى متحرّكا وغير متحرّك، وريحا وغير ريح وهو واحد بالعدد؛ فكان ذلك إبطالا أيضًا.
After what was not; and the nature of the genus is one in number, so it cannot exist in two things differing in essence, namely the mortal and the non-mortal. And among the topics is also one that is the reverse of this, namely making the passion a genus for the affected; and its example in the teaching is that of him who makes the wind moving air and leads to the illusion that its prevention is because the wind is not moving air but rather movement; so the apparent state in it suggests that air should not be made a genus for wind. And if this is taken according to this appearance, then the passion is not made a genus for the affected, but rather the affected is made a genus for the passion, the reason being that its meaning is to make wind a genus for moving air so that air becomes a species of wind. And the interpretation of the example clarifies it, for it was said: 'And it is not permissible that air be wind at all.' That is because air remains one in number as wind and non-wind, while the species does not remain its individual one in number, and it leaves its genus to another genus. So the reason is that wind is made in the example a genus for moving air; but in reality it is an accident in air, because it is a movement in it or because it is moved from it, i.e., from air, as if he said: wind must not be moving air but rather moving [thing] that is air, so that the genus of wind is the moving [thing] and its differentia is that it is from air. Then if the statement of the speaker that air is a species of wind is refuted by other aspects—how can air be a species of wind while wind does not exist other than air?—that does not prevent it from being refuted also from this aspect. And these refutative topics are not like things that have no other topic for refutation; rather, one of the topics is them. So if someone said that air is a species of wind, it could be refuted by saying that air remains moving and non-moving, and wind and non-wind, while being one in number; thus that is also a refutation.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical analysis of a mistaken genus assignment. The author identifies a specific topic (موضع) where one might erroneously make the passion (انفعال) the genus of the affected (منفعل), using the example of wind. The example: someone might claim that wind is moving air, but then argue that wind is not moving air but movement itself. This inverts the relation: instead of making the passion (movement) the genus of the affected (wind), it makes the affected (wind) the genus of the passion (movement), leading to the absurdity that air (a more stable entity) would be a species of wind (a transient phenomenon). The refutation relies on the principle that a genus must be one in number across individuals, whereas air can be both wind and non-wind while remaining the same air, showing that wind is an accident (عرض) in air, not a genus. This ensures that the dialectician does not confuse accidental properties with essential genera.

Key Terms
انفعال
موضع
جنس
منفعل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it invalid to say 'air is a species of wind'?
A) Because wind is a substance and air is an accident.
B) Because air remains one in number while being both wind and non-wind, so wind cannot be its genus.
C) Because wind is a movement and air is a body, so they are in different categories.
D) Because the logician Aristotle did not permit it.

Key Takeaway
An accidental property cannot be a genus because the subject retains its numerical identity when the accident is removed, violating the unity of genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In a debate on the definition of 'sunna', someone claims that 'bid‘a' is a species of 'sunna' because both are actions. Refute this using the principle from the lesson: a genus must be constantly predicable; sunna and bid‘a cannot be genus-species because the subject (action) remains the same but the attribute changes, showing bid‘a is an accident, not a species.
?
2. Identify the fallacy in the statement: 'The heart is a species of blood because both are parts of the body.' Explain why this is analogous to making passion a genus for affected, using the criterion of numerical identity.

Dialectical Genus and Common Opinion
Explains how common opinion can lead to false genus assignments in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real genus and a dialectical genus based on common opinion, using the criteria of necessary concomitance and co-extensiveness.

Core Idea
In dialectic, a predicate is accepted as genus if it is necessary and co-extensive, not necessarily real.

In dialectical reasoning, arguments often rely on commonly accepted opinions (mashhūr). This can lead to treating predicates like 'man' when applied to 'writer' as if they were genera, even though they are not. Understanding the criteria dialecticians use to accept something as a genus in common opinion helps us distinguish genuine definitions from dialectical tools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of common opinion genus
The passage gives examples: 'man' predicated of 'writer', 'sound' of 'letter', 'letter' of 'utterance'. These are not real genera but are treated as such in dialectic.
2
Understand the real genus condition
For a real genus, the term must be predicated of the species (e.g., 'animal' of 'human'), not the other way around. The species cannot be predicated of the genus.
3
Learn the dialectical criterion
The dialectician accepts a predicate as a genus if it is necessary (lāzim) and co-extensive (mun‘akis) with the subject. This is the basis for common opinion genera.
The text states: 'والجدلي يجعل الشيء جنسًا من جهة أنه لازم للشيء ومنعكس عليه'
4
Apply the criterion to the examples
Check if 'writer' is necessary and co-extensive with 'man'. It is not; not all men are writers. So it is only a dialectical genus based on common opinion, not a real genus.
5
Recognize the risk of mistaking common opinion for reality
The passage warns that just because something is described according to common opinion, it does not mean it is described that way in reality. This is a key distinction in dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الموضوعات يصدق على الجملة في المشهور هو مثل الإنسان على الكاتب، والصوت على الحرف، والحرف على اللفظ؛ فإنه يشكل فيها الأمر فيظن أجناسا. والمعلم الأوّل إنما أورد أمثال هذه الأشياء بحسب الشهرة مكان الأجناس (١)، ولا يلزم من كون الشيء متصفًا بالشيء بحسب الشهرة [٣٣٤] أن يكون متصفّا بذلك الشيء في الحقيقة. والشيء لا يمكن أن يكون جنسا للشيء في الحقيقة إلا وأن يكون محمولاً على ذلك الشيء لا موضوعا له. والجدلي يجعل الشيء جنسًا من جهة أنه لازم للشيء ومنعكس عليه؛ فإن ما يكون كذلك فذلك يعد من الأجناس في المشهور. فإذا سلم ذلك فقد ظهر إنتاجه؛ ولا مجال للمنع. وقد يجعل الانعكاس جنسا كذلك لما أنه يعد من الأجناس في المشهور فيجعل المنقسم جنسًا للعدد. وعلى هذا في النظائر فإن من الأشياء ما يكون شبيها بالشيء فيظن أنه يكون ذلك بالحقيقة؛ وذلك قطعي عند الأكثر، ومشهور عندهم. والتعرُّض لذكر كل واحد منها على سبيل التمثيل يفضي إلى التطويل بل إلى التعطيل عند أهل التحصيل.
فصل : في المواضع المخصوصة بالخاصة وأمثالها
فننظر في هذه المواضع أنها أعم من الخاصة المفردة والمركبة والرسم، وأنها هي على ما عرفته من أقسامها أو التي هي كذا وكذا؛ والتي نبحث هنا عن مواضعها فهي الدائمة المساوية التي يعرف بها المجهول. فمن المواضع المعدَّة نحو الخاصة مواضع تشترك في اعتبار واحد؛ وهو أنه فصل وضعت الخاصة جيدة معتبراً فيها الجودة دون الصدق والكذب. وهو اعتبار أنه هل وقع عندما الشيء مجهول فلا يعرف به الشيء، وكل ما هو معروف عندما
Then among the topics that are predicated in general according to common opinion are like 'man' on 'writer', 'sound' on 'letter', and 'letter' on 'utterance'; for the matter becomes problematic in them and they are thought to be genera. And the First Teacher only cited such things according to common opinion in place of genera (1), and it does not follow from a thing being described by a thing according to common opinion that it is described by that thing in reality. And a thing cannot be a genus for a thing in reality unless it is predicated of that thing and not subjected to it. And the dialectician makes a thing a genus in virtue of its being necessary for the thing and co-extensive with it; for whatever is like that is counted among the genera in common opinion. And if that is conceded, then its conclusion appears; and there is no room for denial. And conversion (al-‘aks) may also be made a genus in this way, because it is counted among the genera in common opinion, so the divisible is made a genus for number. And similarly in analogous cases, for among things there are those that resemble a thing and are thought to be that in reality; and that is certain for most people, and well-known among them. And attempting to mention each one of them by way of example leads to prolixity, rather to obstruction for those of attainment.
Chapter: On the Specific Topics Related to Property and Their Likes
We examine these topics, that they are more general than the property (khāṣṣa) whether simple, compound, or description (rasm), and that they are as you have known from their divisions or as they are such and such; and what we investigate here regarding their topics are the perpetual co-extensive ones by which the unknown is known. Among the prepared topics related to property are topics that share in one consideration: namely a differentia (faṣl) when the property is put as good, considering its quality rather than truth and falsity. And it is the consideration whether when the thing is unknown it is not known by the thing, and everything that is known when...

Commentary
This passage discusses how common opinion (shuhra) can lead to mistaking a predicate for a genus in dialectical reasoning. The author gives examples such as 'man' being predicated of 'writer', 'sound' of 'letter', and 'letter' of 'utterance'—these are commonly thought to be genus-species relations but are not truly so. The 'First Teacher' (Aristotle) used such examples based on common opinion. The key point is that for a real genus, the term must be predicated of the species, not the other way around; but in dialectic, if a term is necessary and co-extensive (lāzim mun‘akis), it is accepted as a genus in common opinion. The author then extends this to 'conversion' (al-‘aks) being considered a genus, making the divisible a genus for number. Finally, a new section begins on specific topics related to property (khāṣṣa). The dialectician examines these topics to identify the perpetual co-extensive properties that reveal the unknown, considering the quality of the definition.

A) Because it is predicated of the thing in reality.
B) Because it is necessary and co-extensive with the thing.
C) Because the First Teacher said so.
D) Because it is a substance.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, a predicate is accepted as a genus if it is necessary and co-extensive, even if it is not a real genus.

Properties (Khāṣṣa) in Dialectical Topics
Examines the specific topics related to property (khāṣṣa) and how they are used in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a property (khāṣṣa) in dialectical reasoning and explain its role as a perpetual co-extensive universal that reveals the unknown.

Core Idea
In dialectic, properties are co-extensive universals used to define the unknown, valued for quality not truth.

The text now turns to specific topics related to property (khāṣṣa). These are special dialectical tools: they are perpetual, co-extensive predicates that help identify the unknown. Unlike genera, properties are evaluated by their quality (jūda) rather than their truth or falsity, because in dialectic the goal is often persuasion rather than certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of property in this context
The passage describes properties as 'perpetual co-extensive universals' (الدائمة المساوية) by which the unknown is known.
2
Understand the quality criterion
Properties are considered 'good' based on their quality, not their truth or falsity. This means a dialectician may use a property even if it is not absolutely true, as long as it is useful for identifying the unknown.
The text says: 'فصل وضعت الخاصة جيدة معتبراً فيها الجودة دون الصدق والكذب'
3
Differentiate property from genus
Property (khāṣṣa) is co-extensive with the species, while genus is broader. For example, 'laughter' is a property of 'human' (all humans laugh, but not only humans? Actually, only humans laugh), whereas 'animal' is a genus. In dialectic, both can be topics, but property is specifically for identifying the unknown.
4
Provide an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, the property of 'riba' might be 'exchange of like goods with excess delay' – this property is co-extensive with riba and helps identify new cases of riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Using Opposites and Correlatives to Prove Properties
To focus on opposites and correlatives topoi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the topoi of opposites and correlatives to establish or refute a property in a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Opposites and correlatives mirror each other's property claims.

Dialectical topoi are argumentative patterns that allow you to transfer property claims from one term to its counterpart. The first two topoi in al-Kabir's list exploit the relationships of opposition and correlation. Understanding these will give you a powerful tool for theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relationship type
Determine whether the terms are opposites (ضدان) or correlatives (متضايفان). Opposites are mutually exclusive within the same species, like justice/injustice. Correlatives imply each other relationally, like divisor/quotient.
Be careful: opposites are not the same as contradictories.
2
Formulate the rule
For opposites: if one opposite is not a property of its opposite, then the other opposite is not a property of the other. For correlatives: if one correlate is not a property of its correlate, the other is not a property of the other. The text gives the example: if divisor is not a property of double, then quotient is not a property of half.
The rule works both for affirmation (by contrapositive) and refutation.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the opposites 'halal' and 'haram'. If halal is not a property of a forbidden act, then haram is not a property of a permissible act. This mirrors the text's example of justice and the best thing.
This is a valid dialectical move.
4
Check the direction
The topos from opposites is mentioned as suitable for affirmation (الإثبات), while the topos from correlatives works for both affirmation and refutation. Note that the text says 'suitable for affirmation' for opposites, implying the contrapositive form is valid.
In dialectic, one can also use the topos in the opposite direction by contrapositive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في استعمال المواضع المشتركة في الخاصة منها أنه إذا لم يكن الضد خاصة الضد الآخر فإنه إن لم يكن خاصة / العدل أنه أفضل شيء لم يكن خاصة الجور أنه أحسن شيء؛ وهذا يصلح للإثبات. ومنها ما يكون من المتضايفات مثل أنه إن لم يكن الفاصل خاصة الضعف فليس المفصول خاصة النصف؛ وإنه يصلح للإثبات والإبطال. ومنها ما يكون من العدم والملكة أنه إذا لم يكن عدم الحس خاصة للصمم لم يكن وجود الحس خاصة للسمع ; وإنه يصلح للإثبات والإبطال. ومنها ما يكون من المتناقضات بجهة أنه إن كان المحمول خاصة فمقابله بالنقيض ليست خاصة؛ وهذا للإبطال. ومنها ما يكون من جهة التقابل أيضًا وهو أنه إن كان الشيء خاصة للشيء فلا يكون خاصة لما يقابله بالنقيض؛ وإنه لا يصلح للإثبات إلا على سبيل المغالطة. ومنها ما يكون على سبيل تعادل القسمة من جنس واحد مثل أنه إن كان معقول ومحسوس وثابت وغير ثابت؛ ثم لم يكن الحيوان المحسوس خاصة للمائت لم يكن الحيوان المعقول خاصة لغير المائت نحو الملك. ومنها ما يكون من التصاريف أنه إذا كان المصروف ليس خاصة للمصروف؛ فليس التصريف خاصة للتصريف، وبالعكس؛ وهو موضع جدلي للإثبات والإبطال وتولده أمثلة مثل أنه إن لم يكن خاصة ما هو على طريق العدل أن يكون على طريقه الجميل لم يكن خاصة العدالة الجميل. وإن كان خاصة الإنسان أنه مشاء ذو رجلين كانت خاصة ما يجري على طريق الإنسانية أنه يجري على طريق هذا المشيء. وكذلك يعتبر جانب سلب التصريف مع سلب المصروف مثل ما ليس على طريق العدل وما ليس على طريق الجميل وغير العدل وغير الجميل، وكذلك العدم والملكة والمضاف والضد.
Chapter on using common topoi in property. Among them: if the opposite is not a property of the other opposite, then if justice is not a property of the best thing, injustice is not a property of the worst thing; this is suitable for affirmation. Among them is what comes from correlatives, like: if the divisor is not a property of the double, then the quotient is not a property of the half; it is suitable for affirmation and refutation. Among them is what comes from privation and possession: if absence of sense is not a property of deafness, then existence of sense is not a property of hearing; it is suitable for affirmation and refutation. Among them is what comes from contradictories in one respect: if a predicate is a property, then its contradictory opposite is not a property; this is for refutation. Among them is what comes from the direction of opposition also: that if a thing is a property of something, it is not a property of its contradictory opposite; it is not suitable for affirmation except by way of fallacy. Among them is what comes from equal division of one genus, like: if there is the intelligible and the sensible, the stable and the unstable; then if the sensible animal is not a property of the mortal, then the intelligible animal is not a property of the immortal, like the angel. Among them is what comes from inflections: if the inflected word is not a property of the inflected, then the inflection is not a property of the inflection, and vice versa; it is a dialectical topos for affirmation and refutation, and its examples are generated like: if it is not a property of what is on the way of justice that what is on the way of beauty is its property, then beauty is not a property of justice. And if man's property is that he is a walker with two legs, then the property of what runs on the way of humanity is that it runs on the way of this walking. And likewise the side of negation of inflection with negation of the inflected is considered, like what is not on the way of justice and what is not on the way of beauty, and non-justice and non-beauty, and similarly privation and possession, the relative, and the opposite.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a chapter listing common dialectical topoi (مواضع جدلية) used to establish or refute a property (خاصة). Each topos provides a rule based on a relation (opposition, correlation, etc.). The author first presents topoi from opposites: if one opposite is not a property of another opposite, then the other opposite is not a property of the first. Example: if justice is not a property of the best, then injustice is not a property of the worst. This works for affirmation (by contrapositive). Next, from correlatives (متضايفات): if the divisor is not a property of the double, then the quotient is not a property of the half. This works for both affirmation and refutation. Third, from privation and possession (عدم وملكة): if absence of sense is not a property of deafness, then presence of sense is not a property of hearing. Fourth, from contradictories (متناقضات): if a predicate is a property, its contradictory is not a property; this only refutes. Fifth, a variant of opposition: if something is a property of one thing, it is not a property of its contradictory; this is only valid for refutation, not affirmation (using it for affirmation is fallacious). Sixth, from equal division of a genus (تعادل القسمة): if a genus is divided into two species (e.g., animal into sensible and intelligible), then if one species is not a property of one member of the division, the other species is not a property of the other member (e.g., if sensible animal is not property of mortal, then intelligible animal is not property of immortal like angels). Seventh, from inflections (تصاريف): considering derived words (e.g., justice/just, beauty/beautiful). The rule: if the inflected form (e.g., 'just') is not a property of the base (e.g., 'justice'), then the inflection (e.g., 'justicehood') is not a property of the inflection (e.g., 'justness'), and vice versa. This topos is dialectical, for both affirmation and refutation. Examples are generated by transforming terms, such as: if what is on the way of justice does not have beauty as its property, then beauty is not a property of justice. Similarly, if man's property is 'walker with two legs', then what is on the way of humanity has the property of being on the way of this walking. The topos also considers the negative side: e.g., what is not on the way of justice, etc., and extends to privation/possession, relatives, and opposites.

Key Terms
ضد
متضايفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the topos from correlatives is suitable for:
A) Affirmation only
B) Refutation only
C) Both affirmation and refutation
D) Neither affirmation nor refutation

Key Takeaway
Opposites and correlatives provide reliable patterns for transferring property claims in dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the pair 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'mubah' (permissible). Use the topos of opposites to state: if wajib is not a property of permissible acts, then mubah is not a property of obligatory acts. Is this a valid application of the topos? Explain.
?
Consider the correlatives 'cause' and 'effect'. Using the topos of correlatives, construct an argument about properties of a cause and its effect in the context of theological causation.

Privation/Possession and Contradictories in Dialectic
To focus on privation/possession and contradictories topoi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the topos of privation/possession and the topos of contradictories, and identify which is suitable for affirmation and which for refutation.

Core Idea
Privation/possession topoi work both ways; contradictories only refute.

Two more topoi deal with pairs where one is the absence of the other: privation/possession, and contradictories. While they both involve opposition, their logical behavior differs. Mastering this distinction prevents fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Understand privation/possession
Privation (عدم) is the absence of a capacity (ملكة). For example, deafness is privation of hearing. The rule: if absence of sense is not a property of deafness, then presence of sense is not a property of hearing.
The pair is not arbitrary; privation presupposes a natural possession.
2
Apply the topos of privation/possession
The text says this topos is suitable for both affirmation and refutation. Example: if absence of sense is not a property of deafness, then presence of sense is not a property of hearing. This can be used to argue about properties of senses.
This topos works like the correlatives topos.
3
Understand the topos of contradictories
Contradictories (متناقضات) are propositions that cannot both be true or both false. The rule: if a predicate is a property, its contradictory opposite is not a property. Example: if 'just' is a property of a person, then 'not just' is not a property.
This topos is only for refutation (الإبطال).
4
Note the limitation
The text explicitly states that the topos of contradictories is for refutation only. Using it for affirmation is not valid, because the contrapositive would not hold in dialectical contexts. The variant of opposition (من جهة التقابل) also only works for refutation.
Using it for affirmation would be a fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في استعمال المواضع المشتركة في الخاصة منها أنه إذا لم يكن الضد خاصة الضد الآخر فإنه إن لم يكن خاصة / العدل أنه أفضل شيء لم يكن خاصة الجور أنه أحسن شيء؛ وهذا يصلح للإثبات. ومنها ما يكون من المتضايفات مثل أنه إن لم يكن الفاصل خاصة الضعف فليس المفصول خاصة النصف؛ وإنه يصلح للإثبات والإبطال. ومنها ما يكون من العدم والملكة أنه إذا لم يكن عدم الحس خاصة للصمم لم يكن وجود الحس خاصة للسمع ; وإنه يصلح للإثبات والإبطال. ومنها ما يكون من المتناقضات بجهة أنه إن كان المحمول خاصة فمقابله بالنقيض ليست خاصة؛ وهذا للإبطال. ومنها ما يكون من جهة التقابل أيضًا وهو أنه إن كان الشيء خاصة للشيء فلا يكون خاصة لما يقابله بالنقيض؛ وإنه لا يصلح للإثبات إلا على سبيل المغالطة. ومنها ما يكون على سبيل تعادل القسمة من جنس واحد مثل أنه إن كان معقول ومحسوس وثابت وغير ثابت؛ ثم لم يكن الحيوان المحسوس خاصة للمائت لم يكن الحيوان المعقول خاصة لغير المائت نحو الملك. ومنها ما يكون من التصاريف أنه إذا كان المصروف ليس خاصة للمصروف؛ فليس التصريف خاصة للتصريف، وبالعكس؛ وهو موضع جدلي للإثبات والإبطال وتولده أمثلة مثل أنه إن لم يكن خاصة ما هو على طريق العدل أن يكون على طريقه الجميل لم يكن خاصة العدالة الجميل. وإن كان خاصة الإنسان أنه مشاء ذو رجلين كانت خاصة ما يجري على طريق الإنسانية أنه يجري على طريق هذا المشيء. وكذلك يعتبر جانب سلب التصريف مع سلب المصروف مثل ما ليس على طريق العدل وما ليس على طريق الجميل وغير العدل وغير الجميل، وكذلك العدم والملكة والمضاف والضد.
Chapter on using common topoi in property. Among them: if the opposite is not a property of the other opposite, then if justice is not a property of the best thing, injustice is not a property of the worst thing; this is suitable for affirmation. Among them is what comes from correlatives, like: if the divisor is not a property of the double, then the quotient is not a property of the half; it is suitable for affirmation and refutation. Among them is what comes from privation and possession: if absence of sense is not a property of deafness, then existence of sense is not a property of hearing; it is suitable for affirmation and refutation. Among them is what comes from contradictories in one respect: if a predicate is a property, then its contradictory opposite is not a property; this is for refutation. Among them is what comes from the direction of opposition also: that if a thing is a property of something, it is not a property of its contradictory opposite; it is not suitable for affirmation except by way of fallacy. Among them is what comes from equal division of one genus, like: if there is the intelligible and the sensible, the stable and the unstable; then if the sensible animal is not a property of the mortal, then the intelligible animal is not a property of the immortal, like the angel. Among them is what comes from inflections: if the inflected word is not a property of the inflected, then the inflection is not a property of the inflection, and vice versa; it is a dialectical topos for affirmation and refutation, and its examples are generated like: if it is not a property of what is on the way of justice that what is on the way of beauty is its property, then beauty is not a property of justice. And if man's property is that he is a walker with two legs, then the property of what runs on the way of humanity is that it runs on the way of this walking. And likewise the side of negation of inflection with negation of the inflected is considered, like what is not on the way of justice and what is not on the way of beauty, and non-justice and non-beauty, and similarly privation and possession, the relative, and the opposite.

Equal Division Topos: Transferring Property across Species
To focus on equal division topos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the topos of equal division (ta'adul al-qismah) to generate arguments about properties of coordinate species.

Core Idea
Equal division mirrors property claims from one species to its coordinate.

When a genus is divided into two mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive species, the property of one member of the division can be transferred to the other member through the topos of equal division. This is a powerful tool for argumentation in fields where classification is central.

Guided Path
1
Identify the equal division
Find a genus that is divided into two species that are mutually exclusive and cover the genus completely. For example, animal is divided into sensible (has sense perception) and intelligible (does not have sense perception). Another example: acts are divided into wajib and mubah.
The division must be exhaustive and exclusive.
2
State the rule
If one species is not a property of one member of the division, then the other species is not a property of the other member. The text's example: if sensible animal is not a property of the mortal, then intelligible animal is not a property of the immortal (like angels).
The rule works like a contrapositive transfer.
3
Work through the example
The division of animal into sensible and intelligible. Mortal animals are a subset of sensible animals. If the property 'sensible animal' is not true of mortal, then 'intelligible animal' is not true of immortal. This shows how property claims transpose across the division.
The topos is based on the principle of complementarity.
4
Generate your own
Think of another equal division in Islamic sciences. For instance, rulings (ahkam) are divided into wajib and mubah. Using the topos, if wajib is not a property of prayer, then mubah is not a property of fasting. This can be used in juristic arguments.
The topos works both for affirmation and refutation, as the passage implies.

Inflectional Topos: Deriving Properties from Word Forms
To focus on inflection topos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the topos of inflections to argue about properties based on derived word forms.

Core Idea
Word derivations mirror property relationships across grammatical forms.

The final topos in this chapter draws on linguistic inflections: how words are derived from roots. This topos is distinctly Arabic and shows the integration of logic with grammar. It is dialectical and works for both affirmation and refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the topos rule
The rule: if the inflected word (المصروف/المشتق) is not a property of the base (المصدر/الأصل), then the inflection (التصريف/الاشتقاق) is not a property of the inflection (the derived form), and vice versa. In simpler terms: if the derivative is not a property of the root, then the act of derivation is not a property of the derivative itself.
This is a symmetrical topos; it works both ways.
2
Parse the example
The text gives an indirect example: if 'just' (عادل) is not a property of 'justice' (عدل), then 'justicehood' is not a property of 'justness'. In Arabic, the topos uses the derived terms 'على طريق العدل' and 'على طريق الجميل'.
The topos is about the relationship between the base form and the derived form.
3
Apply to a negative example
The topos also works with negations: 'what is not on the way of justice' and 'what is not on the way of beauty', as well as 'non-justice' and 'non-beauty'. This shows the topos extends to privative terms.
The topos covers both positive and negative sides.
4
Use in Islamic sciences
In tafsir, one might argue about the properties of derived words from the same root. For example, the words 'kafara' (to disbelieve), 'kufr' (disbelief), 'kafir' (disbeliever). If the property of 'kafir' does not hold for a certain person, then 'kufr' does not hold for that person's deeds. This topos allows transferring properties across inflections.
This topos is particularly useful in Arabic grammar and rhetoric.
